content: 'The intention of this note is to become a blog post:


  ## Raw Recording


  Okay, this is the oration for a blog post, maybe an open letter of sorts, as I am
  just wrestling with my new job and the responsibilities that either are put on me
  or that I''ve put on myself as the world is changing.

  And software development and software engineering and code writing

  is just changing, thanks to AI, thanks to agents and their introduction into the
  corporate environment.

  The internet is full of opinions and ideas that push and pull and prod people in
  different directions.

  And every one of those ideas has contexts, which is necessary to keep in mind when
  filtering the ideas.

  At a smaller startup where you can have a $200 a month Claude Code subscription
  for all of your engineers, that''s gonna

  yield certain results that''s going to encourage and invite certain people into
  that space to use those tools, the clawed tools, or Gemini or Codex.

  I work at a big company, Caterpillar.

  We have a GitHub co-pilot, but through co-pilot we have access to the kind of frontier
  large language models.

  From there, we have access to the co-pilot harness, whether it''s co-pilot CLI or
  the co-pilot integration with Visual Studio code, or the other IDEs like JetBrains,
  et cetera.

  Out there in the world, he has certain opinions from some people.

  You have people who are very pro agents like Steve Yeage, who has been a big influence
  to me over the last several months, as I''ve definitely jumped into the deep end
  of agentic coding.

  I haven''t even really opened my IDD in months, except for small things that I want
  to do by hand.

  enterprise grade work for the last three months or so and maybe a little longer
  has been fully written and produced by agents and i''ve been using AI to write code
  to some capacity or another since chad gpt came out really since like claude sonnet
  3.5 but this thinking that i am doing is

  It''s really supposed to be about me, so this blog post I want to write is me processing
  thoughts on the expectation change that''s placed on developers in certain environments
  for certain types of work.

  I guess even that, like that sounds kind of vague.

  So I can only speak to myself.

  So I need to put a big annotation and plaster this blog post with this big caveat
  message that this is me, this is my experience, this is my position, and this is
  not a truth that is wide reaching.

  It''s not any kind of prediction on the, you know,

  the larger change happening in the world.

  This is just where I am at.

  So maybe for the post to make sense, I need to give a brief amount of history about
  me and my work.

  I''m not a trained software engineer, but I''ve been, I guess I''ve been a pretty
  impressive asset in my employment.

  I''ve been at Caterpillar for nine years, and in that nine years or over in the
  last several years, I''ve moonlit, moonlighted a little bit.

  And at every place I''ve done any other amount of work, I''ve just consistently
  impressed people.

  Now, this really just leads me to have more imposter syndrome because I know or
  I have learned what impresses people,

  And it isn''t necessarily excellence, although I try to do things well and I want
  to solve problems and maintain the systems that I build to solve those problems.

  But I don''t write the best code.

  I''m not the smartest developer.

  I don''t know the most.

  I get to know the things I work with and I learn a lot and I work fast because I
  have

  Not a super unique, but a rare ability to focus in, when the stars align, to focus
  and produce more than the next person.

  Not every next person, but a lot of the next people, a lot of the people I work
  with, I outperform.

  And that''s been true, or it has seemed true, from every review, pretty much every
  review I''ve ever received, that''s been true.

  And as I''ve had that just personal truth be reflected in my work, it''s been enhanced
  with AI because I''ve been an early adopter and even as a, even as somewhat of a
  skeptic, like I''ve tried to maintain a posture of skepticism.

  But since Opus 4.5,

  Even though I might talk like a skeptic, I am leaning into agents hard.

  And I am practicing my own harness engineering with my nexus project.

  And I''m using it to do engineering work at Caterpillar that''s going into production
  for us, which is like data platform type of work.

  It''s not custom, it''s not external customer facing.

  It''s internal tooling, which is what I mostly built.

  And the thing I''m really trying to wrap my head around, or I guess I''m trying
  to explain, is without AI, I was out producing a lot of people.

  And with AI, you can produce just a shit ton of code.

  And I''m trying to do that part well, but in my own gifts and curses of hyper focus
  and

  Wanting to meet expectations and solve problems, I''m jumping into the new frontier
  very quickly.

  But something I''ve noticed is that as I''ve produced a lot, people are just impressed.

  And they''re not necessarily impressed with the excellence.

  They''re impressed with how I talk about things.

  They''re impressed with how much I do, and they''re impressed with the output they
  see.

  But the result of a bunch of code isn''t all of the code itself.

  And I haven''t read all the code I''ve produced with agents.

  I''ve certainly tried to test most of it or had most of it tested.

  But as I''ve sought to do this and as I''ve been working in this capacity of using
  agents very heavily, I just feel kind of torn.

  in trusting the agents and in working with the agents in the ways that I think are
  appropriate, but also collaborating with other humans who are also using AI.

  But are they using AI in a trustworthy way?

  I don''t know, and I''m not sure how to validate it.

  But my managers seem to think I know how to validate it, and they seem impressed
  with the work I do, and they seem impressed with my understanding of AI and agentic
  coding.

  And so I feel all of this pressure to continue to be a top performer like I have
  been since before AI.

  I feel a lot of pressure to, I don''t want to coach people in how I do things, but
  I''m being asked to coach people and how I do things because other people I work
  with notice me.

  And I guess I''m almost, I''m just kind of tired of being noticed.

  Because being noticed is not resulting in me becoming rich.

  And money is not the most important thing.

  But man, I''d love to just make enough money to fuck off for the rest of my life
  and never look at a computer ever again.

  I would love...

  to not have to continue to maintain systems that I build or that other people build
  or to collaborate with people I don''t know, who might not even be long term.

  That''s the one thing about Caterpillar is I''m working with a lot of contractors.

  And nothing wrong with contracting, I''ve done that.

  Nothing wrong with being a contractor, but

  They could disappear at any moment, and all the code that they write, and even today,
  all the code that they prompt and have an agent right becomes my problem, my maintenance
  issue, my responsibility.

  And it just is,

  It''s just making me nervous to maintain and to understand and to continue to work
  under conditions that I can''t even tell if I''m making them for myself or if they''re
  real expectations.

  Like if I fell off the face of the earth or if I dropped agentic coding entirely
  and I just went back to coding by hand completely, would my output continue to impress
  people today?

  I don''t know the answer to that.

  But I certainly wouldn''t be solving problems as fast, but would I be creating less
  problems?

  Like I''ve solved a lot of problems in three months in my new role here with Forge.

  But I don''t know how many problems I''ve created.

  Only time will tell.

  And the thing is, the guys I work with who are contractors, they might not be around
  long enough for time to tell, but because I''m not rich yet, I probably will be
  long enough, or be around long enough.

  to experience those bugs and those problems that I don''t know about yet.

  And I am just feeling so much pressure to be the best and to be the one to solve
  problems.

  And I don''t know if I''m putting it on me or if cat''s putting it on me or if my
  project manager is putting it on me.

  I''m just really struggling with the amount of responsibility I feel.

  And I''m feeling frustrated about it because that amount of responsibility feels
  like it has scaled a lot with agents, with AI, but not my paycheck.

  And in fact, my paycheck has gone down quite a bit.

  And I''m not more relaxed.

  I''m making about half of what I''ve made over the last couple of years.

  And I''m not more relaxed.

  I don''t feel freer.

  I don''t feel like I have 50% of the responsibility that I did.

  Cal Autonomy awoke something in me for purpose, but that feels like it''s already
  starting to die.

  I''m already tired.

  I''m, I don''t know about burnt out, but I''m tired.

  I was reviewing some code that one of these contractors I''m working with.

  It''s actually only an intern.

  He''s not even a contractor.

  He''s an intern.

  And I''m reviewing a pull request of his.

  And damn, it''s a lot of code.

  And it looks somewhat useful.

  But I don''t know exactly how to test it.

  That''s not true actually, I do know how to test it, but I don''t own enough of
  our infrastructure at Caterpillar to test it easily.

  So do I trust him?

  Do I trust his prompt?

  Do I trust his harness?

  Do I trust the tests that I have already put in the code base to capture and block
  any breakages he might have introduced with this code for the code I''ve already
  been producing?

  It''s different when I''m intimately familiar with the code base.

  And I''ve been, I don''t know, definitely two other places at least where I had
  very intimate familiarity with the system and with the code and with the platform.

  But the one I''m on right now is new, and I built it.

  I built it with agents.

  I built it to replace a thing that was never even working.

  But I built it.

  It''s mine.

  It''s not somebody else''s.

  It''s not that we''ll document it.

  There''s a lot of documentation, but it''s all been agent produced, and even though
  I''ve reviewed it, it''s hard for me to organize it in the right way in a way that
  makes sense.

  I''m just feeling so frustrated that it seems like, as the tools are getting better,
  as developers are able to move faster, it feels like I am expected to move faster.

  but I''m not expected to make any more money.

  I''m not expected to be compensated anymore for the increase in velocity and responsibility.

  It''s almost like if I could produce, this metric is not gonna be real, but let''s
  just, for the sake of argument, make it up.

  If I can write 30,000 lines of code in a year and make $100,000, I feel fine about
  that, whatever that number means.

  But now I can actually produce 300,000 lines of code in a year.

  I''m definitely producing 10 times more code than normal.

  I don''t know if I''m doing 10 times more engineering, but I am making exactly the
  same amount of money.

  And so why did I do it?

  Why am I leaning into it so hard?

  Why am I trying to still be the front runner to be on the bleeding edge when caterpillar
  is not going to pay anymore?

  And I could go somewhere else.

  I could go to Avant.

  I could make more.

  It''s a system I''m familiar with.

  It''s even people I know.

  And there''s AI there too.

  But it''s a system I know.

  It''s a system I know how to support and maintain.

  And there''s not an expectation that I''m going to produce 10,000 lines of code
  this week.

  But right now at Caterpillar, it feels like I am expected to write 10,000 lines
  of code this week.

  And deploy it and maintain it and solve new problems.

  All in an incredibly political and hectic, chaotic environment where there isn''t
  an appropriate amount of leadership over me.

  I am the expert in AI in my group.

  And that feels retarded.

  I am the professional agentic engineer and that is just scary because I''m not good
  enough to be that.

  I''m not Steve Yeagie, I''m not Simon Willison, I haven''t been a developer for
  20 years.

  I''m just a guy who learned some Python to solve some problems and happen to work
  faster than a lot of people and that reputation has really

  It''s treated me well for sure.

  And my disposition to problem solving has, up until recently, I felt like I had
  been, like I''ve been compensated well by my company, but then I''ve also been able
  to do more work outside of one job for me to feel the value of my own skills.

  But now with AI changing everything,

  Can I show you what''s on the back of my story?

  It feels like I''ve given up the personal gains.

  One second, Alia.

  So I''ve given up personally realizing the benefit of my own skill.

  And now I am like in a position where

  Caterpillar, and only Caterpillar, is reaping the benefit of my new scheme in agentic
  engineering.

  And I think that''s just ticking me off because I stayed with Catt to do something
  good for the world.

  And I think we can do something good for the world.

  But I''m tired.


  ## Notes and Summarization


  ## Possible Titles


  - Agents, Responsibility, and the Cost of Going Faster

  - The Irony of 10x

  - More Output, Less Upside

  - When AI Made Me Faster and Poorer

  - Being Crushed by Responsibility


  ## Working Angle


  This should probably be a personal essay, not a manifesto about AI. The core tension:


  - agents made me dramatically more productive

  - that productivity increased the responsibility I carry

  - the financial upside I used to realize personally has gone down, not up


  ## Section Stub: Opening Scene


  Prompt:


  - Start with a concrete moment. Maybe reviewing a massive PR from the intern / contractor
  side of the system.

  - What did I feel in that moment: confusion, dread, pressure, responsibility, skepticism?

  - What was the specific thought: "I might own all of this later, even if I did not
  really produce or validate all of it myself."

  - Can I open with one sharp sentence that names the tension right away?


  Possible opener idea:


  "Agentic coding has made me faster than ever, but it has also made me feel responsible
  for more than I can honestly hold in my head."


  ### My Opener


  Agentic coding has been an exciting change to me over the last couple of months

  specifically. I''ve been using AI tools for a few years now but something really

  shifted with Opus 4.5 as well as the tools/harnesses getting better and more

  useful around the same time. I''ve been influenced by people like Simon Willison

  and Steve Yegge who have been on the forefront of agentic coding and [vibe

  engineering](https://simonwillison.net/2025/Oct/7/vibe-engineering/) since the

  dawn of ChatGPT and in my small circles of work I''m definitely on the bloodiest

  bleeding edge of the adoption of these practices. Are they the future? I don''t

  know - I tried to maintain a skeptical posture but until the bubble pops it''s

  looking like this is at least a direction the future of my line of work is

  going.


  The tough part is mixing the new world of agentic coding with

  developers on wildly different points in the spectrum of adoption and maturity.

  Here''s a short anecdote about what I mean...


  In a new project that I''m on, I''ve refactored/reimplemented a lot of legacy

  code, producing about 140k lines of code, configuration, and

  docs in about 2 months. It''s an insane amount of "product" and I''ve done it

  entirely with agents. But I didn''t do it with a handful of vanilla chat

  sessions like "Hey Copilot, reimplement this API, no mistakes". I''ve been building

  out my own process for using LLMs effectively. I have no actual idea besides my

  own experience if what I''m building is useful, but it feels pretty good -

  agents for planning, building, reviewing, testing, etc. My "harness" includes

  specialized agents and opinionated development workflows

  to try to ensure that code is never one-shotted into production.


  The catch though is that I''m working with a handful of developers and they are
  not

  early-adopters or aggressive experimenters with these new agentic tools. Most

  of them are still in Steve Yegge''s stage 2 or 3 of AI coding as he''s outlined

  [in this medium

  article introducing

  GasTown](https://steve-yegge.medium.com/welcome-to-gas-town-4f25ee16dd04). They

  open a chat session, and say yes or no to . I''ve been in-between stages

  6 and 7 for a while now - managing multiple agentic sessions that themselves

  run specialized subagents primiarly for context management, although I don''t

  feel quite ready for full blown stage 8 (agents running agnets running agents).

  So this isn''t me saying that I''m

  better than the guys I''m working with or anything, just the project we''re on

  together is now being touched by people on wildly different ends of the agentic

  coding spectrum.


  Finally the anecdote - I''m getting PRs from guys in stages 2 and 3,

  thousands of lines of code and config, that I know they are not necessarily

  experts in, but they''re producing that code with fairly vanilla practices. How

  do I verify it? I know my own prompts, I know what agents generated my code, I know

  the direction and prodding I gave as they''re building, but all

  I know in a PR review from someone else is the diff - not how they tested it

  (unless of course there''s tests, and there must be, but tests are only as good

  as the tests are...), not the steering they gave, not the manual UAT validation

  they did... And ultimately I own the code they merge because chances are I

  outlast their contract with the team. It''s kind of a scary thing to review and

  accept... do I trust my

  agentic practices to validate their work? I genuinely don''t know, and I also

  literally can''t sleep at night.


  ## Section Stub: This Is About Me, Not Everyone


  Prompt:


  - Add a strong caveat early: this is not prediction, prescription, or universal
  truth.

  - Name my context: big company, internal platform work, political environment, mixed
  team ownership, frontier models via Copilot.

  - What about my temperament matters here? Hyperfocus, initiative, problem solving,
  pressure sensitivity, desire to meet expectations.

  - What would I want a reader to not mishear?


  ### My section about me


  I want to make sure to reiterate the context of this post and my thoughts -

  it''s really about me. I''m not saying anything here is true for anyone else, I''m

  not making predictions about tomorrow, and I''m not a prominent FOSS developer or
  a

  high-profile ex-FAANG engineer. I work at a big company, not a fast-paced

  startup. I work on internal tooling, nothing that should even be seen outside

  our network. I''m also a self-taught developer, not a trained software engineer.


  As far as AI goes, lately I''m mostly all in on Github Copilot

  CLI with a sprinkling of Opencode. I''m not using Claude Code or Codex which

  have seem to have their own communities around plugins and usage.


  And since we''re so focused on me right now, the important thing to keep as the

  backdrop for this post is my temperament and make-up. I''m definitely made a

  certain kind of way, not different than every single person necessarily, but

  different enough, from enough people, to not blend in with the 9-5 guy who can

  log out and not think about work until the morning (I''m not saying that''s a

  strength either, my boundary problems are for another time).


  I feel an aggressive burden to solve problems and own those solutions, call it

  white-knighting or a savior complex if you want, but I''ve got enough of a

  reputation at Caterpillar now (and anywhere else I''ve worked) to become a go-to

  person for more than I think is necessarily appropriate. And with that burden

  I''ll bring results - if a problem hooks into my brain it. will. be. solved. I

  probably won''t do the best most clean-code solution right out of the gate, but

  I''ll do whatever I can to solve it.


  ## Section Stub: Who I Was Before Agents


  Prompt:


  - Briefly describe my work history and self-understanding.

  - I am not formally trained, so what made me valuable anyway?

  - What does it mean that people were impressed before AI too?

  - Pull in relevant threads from `pages/blog/forge-ahead.md`: prior projects, practical
  problem solving, being useful, learning systems by doing.

  - What gifts do I think God gave me that made me effective before this shift?


  Questions to answer:


  - What kind of worker was I before agents entered my workflow?

  - What did I already know how to do well?

  - What part of my value came from focus rather than brilliance?


  ### My section about me before agents


  That is who I was before agents made code easy to produce. My first boss at

  Caterpillar used the phrase "tenacious learner" to describe me in several

  reviews. I kind of rejected the description because I basically refused to

  believe that I was really any different, any harder of a worker, than my peers.

  But with almost a decade of experience in the corporate world, and some adult

  perspective on my life, I think it''s accurate... I am a harder worker, to my

  detriment sometimes and I need to put up some firmer boundaries. I''m not

  brilliant but I can focus for a long time in the right circumstances. The gift

  of perseverence (or the curse of not being able to let something go, depending

  on how you look at it) has led to blessing in my life in both reward and skill.


  And then AI came along and with another set of the right circumstances

  catalyzed a new way to work.


  ## Section Stub: Why I Leaned Into Agentic Coding


  Prompt:


  - Explain that I did not stumble into this accidentally; I chose to go hard.

  - Why did it feel natural to me to adopt agents aggressively?

  - What about the mission at Caterpillar Autonomy made the leap feel worth it?

  - Pull in `pages/blog/cat-autonomy-2-0.md`: I stayed because the work felt meaningful,
  not merely because it was interesting.

  - Mention harness engineering / Nexus / experimentation if it helps show this was
  a deliberate investment.


  Questions to answer:


  - Why did I trust this direction enough to reorganize my work around it?

  - Was I chasing novelty, usefulness, recognition, mission, or all of the above?


  ### My section on leaning into agents


  I jumped onto experimenting with AI coding tools as soon as they became

  available, but mostly just tried vibe coding rather than using tools for real

  engineering work. I vibed up an API at one of my jobs that went into production

  way too early, with far too little validation, and it was scary to support it

  from then on out. That was before

  agents were quite as useful as they can be now, but that experience along with

  a handful of other stepping stones began to give me confidence in using the AI

  as a tool, like my IDE is a tool, for producing **solutions** that take the form
  of

  code.


  As one-shot apps got better and better, and as I learned about scoping work

  more appropriately for agentic tools my confidence in them grew. There''s quite

  a difference between "Claude make me a todo app, no mistakes" and scoping out a

  solution in natural language, with some technical guardrails, and having agents

  tackle the implementation methodically.


  ## Section Stub: What Actually Changed


  Prompt:


  - Describe the practical change in my day-to-day work.

  - I am no longer mainly writing code line by line; I am directing, reviewing, testing,
  and integrating generated work.

  - What changed about the quantity of output?

  - What changed about how coworkers and managers perceived me?

  - What changed about the kind of questions people started asking me?


  Questions to answer:


  - When did I realize this was not just autocomplete, but a new mode of working?

  - What does "enterprise-grade work fully written and produced by agents" actually
  mean in practice?


  ### My section on what actually changed


  What''s actually changed for me is quite a lot... I haven''t opened my IDE to

  seriously write code for months now. I''ve oscillated between Opencode and

  Copilot CLI, leaning moreso into Copilot since it''s an approved tool at work

  and as of mid-February is quite good. Mentally I''m approaching problems with a

  little more thought on the front-end than before because prior to agents I

  would think as I implemented. At the scale of work that I do, this was really

  fine - working on CLI utilities to solve simple problems, developing an

  iterative testing cycle for each problem that allowed me to move fast, and once

  I found a groove I was cooking. But now I don''t even need to find it, I open

  Opencode or Copilot CLI with my Planner agent, describe what I want to happen and
  have

  Opus or GPT scope out a plan for me. Usually there''s some back and forth on

  feature scoping, then I review a markdown file it produces, and once it looks

  decent enough to me I say "go" and it goes.


  That works a lot better than I even care to admit because at the same time as

  I''ve been leaning harder into agents, I''ve been building my own harness of

  sorts - not a replacement for Copilot CLI or a competitor to Opencode, but

  moreso an opinionated workflow spine that I force agents into, oh and I''m

  defining the agents, to give strict gates to the SDLC (software development

  lifecycle). Plan and implement is fine for a lot of things, and I do think it''s

  only getting better. My harness, mentioned a few times around here before,

  called Nexus, is a set of agents and rules that I want the code I''ll be

  responsible for to go through before it lands in production. That cycle isn''t

  too complex, and there''s only about 10,000 similar tools to Nexus on Github

  trending right now. I''ve thought about dropping my idea and picking up

  something more popular, like

  [superpowers](https://www.github.com/obra/superpowers) but at the moment I''m

  continuing to develop on and lean into my own idea here. I keep saying a blog

  post is coming, but the high level of Nexus is that it''s a task

  tracker with a CLI that agents use to advance a ticket through a plan -> build

  -> test -> review -> verify -> merge lifecycle that is almost exactly how I

  would otherwise have solved a problem by hand. I think it needs work, I need to

  be harder on TDD methodlogies with agents, and work on verification gating a

  bit more (shoutout to [showboat](https://github.com/simonw/showboat) by Simon

  Willison) but overall it''s a system of thinking that I already participate in

  so I''m doing my best to farm out specific parts of my workflow to agents rather

  than trying to one-shot enterprise problems and solutions.


  I''ve noticed that as I''ve developed Nexus out though, I lean on the agents for

  more and more of my own thinking, and am trusting my problem solving

  **process** moreso than my actual problem solving abilities.


  ## Section Stub: The Hidden Cost of Speed


  Prompt:


  - Write about the gap between output and understanding.

  - I can ship more, but I do not always know the code as intimately as hand-written
  systems I built before.

  - Testing is not the same as ownership.

  - Documentation existing is not the same as confidence.

  - What kinds of future problems does speed create?


  Questions to answer:


  - What do I know I have not fully read?

  - What kinds of bugs or maintenance traps am I afraid I am creating?

  - What does it feel like to build something that works but still feels partially
  foreign?


  ### My section on hidden cost


  The cost of this increase in speed is a lack of familiarity - and the fallout

  of lack of familiarity is hard to express. There''s also many facets to it.

  For me, the first facet is that Nexus helps me move fast, but as I''ve leaned

  into it for more and more of

  the planning, I''m less and less familiar with the state of the code. I find

  myself asking my reviewer agents in fresh sessions often to explain it to me,

  and thankfully they''re usually consistent, but nonetheless I''m still not

  intimtaely familiar with the code. And on Nexus it''s not a big deal, that''s low
  stakes, it''s just me and my workflow.


  I''m using Nexus + Copilot at work and that feels like higher

  stakes... I have my agents explain the status of our project and although they''re

  also somewhat consistent the thing that''s scary is that other people are

  working on that repo with me, and that''s where another layer of complexity

  manifests itself. If its'' just me and my clankers, let''s go all day long,

  rebuild, ask questions, etc... but I have other developers I rub shoulders with

  now, and if they ask me a question what am I going to say? "Hold on, let me prompt

  my agent for you" - it''s LMGTFY on steroids. And the burden becomes if I

  feel like I can own and support what those other developers push into the repo.


  ## Section Stub: Responsibility Without Clear Boundaries


  Prompt:


  - This may be the emotional center before the money section.

  - Describe the contractor / intern dynamic and why it intensifies the burden.

  - Their output can become my long-term maintenance problem.

  - The organization sees me as someone who understands AI and agents, so responsibility
  pools onto me.

  - Ask whether the pressure is explicit or self-imposed, but note that the body feels
  it either way.


  Questions to answer:


  - Who is actually accountable when AI-assisted code breaks six months from now?

  - What does it mean to be treated like the local expert when I do not feel like
  one?

  - Why is being noticed beginning to feel costly instead of rewarding?


  ### My section on responsibility


  Why do I own their work? Well for the third time, this is about me, and my

  situation is that the other developers I work with presently are all contractors.

  Their work agreement with Cat could end at any second, for practically any

  reason. That''s technically true for me as well, seeing as how Cat is an

  "at-will" employer (they can fire anyone for no reason at any time). But the

  incentive structure isn''t there for these guys who technically work for an

  agency... their bonuses aren''t bigger (or even exist) if Cat performs well,

  there''s no extra vacation days in it for em (aye, contractors don''t get

  vacation days anyways), and not that it''s a personal problem - but they''re

  basically mercenaries out to the highest bidder and I happen to know of

  **multiple times where Cat lost a good person to a higher bidder**.


  So this isn''t really me trying to be negative about contractors at all, I''m

  here for a pay-check as well but Cat at least gives me SOME incentive to work

  hard with the goal of compensation regardless of how altruistic I feel in my

  own circumstances.


  It''s also not all about the fact that the other guys aren''t on Cat''s primary

  payroll, I''ve dug myself quite a hole over the last 8-10 years, gaining a

  reputation that I think many would appreciate, but for me only increases the

  stress. I dont'' need to parrot every accolade I''ve ever received, that''s not

  the point, but to make the point as clear as I can - I have a

  lot of respect from quite a few people at Caterpillar. I''m blessed to have that

  reputation, and it''s not like I haven''t worked hard for it - but people talk

  about me in a way so flattering I feel like the main character in a fictional

  story sometimes.


  In a fictional story I can check out the ending, hit up spark notes, or ask AI

  how it ends... but there isn''t an "end" in my real world scenario, there''s only

  tomorrow and I feel the pressure of not knowing what tomorrow holds now more

  than ever.


  Being noticed is starting to feel more costly than rewarding...


  ## Section Stub: The Financial Irony


  Prompt:


  - State this clearly and concretely.

  - For years, I realized more value from my output because I worked two jobs concurrently.

  - Due to the new role, new responsibility, and the demands of agentic coding in
  this environment, I had to go down to one job.

  - So the irony is: the tools make me dramatically more productive, but I personally
  realize less economic upside from my own gifts and skills than I did before.

  - Keep this grounded and honest, not whiny.


  Questions to answer:


  - What exactly have I lost financially?

  - Why does this feel bigger than "I want more money"?

  - Where do the gains of AI go when an individual contributor gets faster?

  - If I can now produce 10x more code, why does my life not feel lighter, freer,
  or better compensated?


  Possible thesis sentence:


  "Agentic coding increased the value I create at work, while decreasing the share
  of that value I personally get to realize."


  ### My section on cost


  What''s the cost? It''s hard to get specific without writing a novel but here''s

  the TLDR - because I''ve been pretty good at what I do I''ve been able to do this

  type of work outside my normal 9-5 responsibilities and with that extra work

  has been some pretty great financial benefits. However with Cat changes,

  responsibility increases, and now owning code that others (and their clankers)

  write, the extra time I gained for myself is eaten-up and has been reclaimed by

  the mega-corp... "Exceeding expectations" every year just meant the bar is

  raised, the expectations are higher, the time-commitment requirement is higher,

  and as I''ve had to meet the requirements of both the new world and the curse of

  being noticed, I''ve lost the time for the extra work... For years I''ve realized

  the benefit of my own skills and drive, but the irony of agents (and a handful

  of other things) is that with the dramatic increase in expectations, not only

  on me but on those I work with and therefore their output, I don''t get to

  realize the benefits of my own gifts anymore.


  ## Section Stub: Mission, Meaning, and Fatigue


  Prompt:


  - Reaffirm the good: this is meaningful work, and that matters to me.

  - Tie back to autonomy, safety, and the reasons I stayed at Caterpillar.

  - Then tell the truth: meaning does not cancel fatigue.

  - What has already started to feel tired, dulled, or overdrawn in me?


  Questions to answer:


  - What purpose did this role awaken in me?

  - Why does that sense of purpose already feel threatened?

  - How do I talk honestly about fatigue without pretending I am fully burned out
  if I am not sure?


  ### My section on meaning


  I feel very torn because the work I''ve been called into with Cat is good, I

  said in [[cat-autonomy-2-0]] that autonomy will save people''s lives. I love

  getting to participate in that mission, it''s the primary reason I didn''t jump

  ship to try to maintain the levels, and type, of work I was doing before... But
  in a few

  short months the mission is being drowned out by expectations and requirements

  that are so high I''m losing the grip on my own life.


  ## Section Stub: Questions I Cannot Yet Answer


  Prompt:


  - Let the essay breathe here instead of forcing a neat conclusion.

  - Gather the real unresolved questions.

  - If I stopped using agents, would I still impress people?

  - Would I solve fewer problems or create fewer future ones?

  - Am I carrying real expectations or anticipated ones?

  - Is this making me a better engineer, or just a faster source of output and responsibility?


  ### My section on questions


  That leads me to questions that I can''t answer, the question I ask daily now of

  "What about tomorrow?". What will agents do for us tomorrow, what problems will

  be solved, what bugs will I create (by agents of course because I''ve never

  written a bug by hand in my whole life \s). If I stopped using agents would

  people still be impressed? Would it even matter?


  ## Section Stub: Conclusion


  Prompt:


  - End modestly, not with a manifesto.

  - I am not anti-AI.

  - I am not saying agents are bad.

  - I am trying to tell the truth about what this transition has cost me personally.

  - Land on the mismatch between speed, responsibility, and reward.


  Possible closing direction:


  - The tools are real.

  - The gains are real.

  - The responsibility is real.

  - The redistribution of value is also real.

  - I am still trying to understand what faithfulness looks like inside that tension.


  ### My conclusion


  I''m certainly not anti-AI, it''s typing all my code. I''m not anti-collaboration,
  although I do wish I could work alone with just my clanker-army to worry about.
  I''m not sure what I am anymore though... AI has changed how I work, what I work
  on, and who I work with... everything has changed in such a short period of time
  and like the ending of this post, it''s pretty jarring.


  Death comes to us all - James Acaster.

  Thanks for reading.


  ## Material To Weave In


  - My lack of formal software engineering background

  - My history of being useful by solving practical problems quickly

  - My tendency toward hyperfocus and over-responsibility

  - The shift from hand-written confidence to agent-mediated uncertainty

  - The contractor / intern maintenance concern

  - The drop from two incomes to one

  - The irony that Cat benefits more from my increased output than I do

  - The Christian framing around calling, providence, stewardship, and fatigue


  ## Things To Avoid


  - sounding like I am making universal claims about all AI use

  - sounding bitter without being specific

  - centering line-count metrics too much without translating them into responsibility

  - pretending I have fully landed on a conclusion

  - making this a complaint post about coworkers instead of a reflection on burden
  and change'

    type='module'>\n// All available pages from Markata\n    // const pages =  markata.map(\"{'slug':slug,'title':title,'description':description,'tags':tags}\",
    filter=config.didyoumean_filter, sort='True')|tojson;\n    // fetch pages from
    config.output_dir / didyoumean.json\n\n    const pages = await fetch('/didyoumean.json').then(response
    => response.json());\n    const populate_search_input = false\n    const search_hotkey
    = \"/\"\n\n// Get current path from URL, removing leading/trailing slashes\n    if
    (populate_search_input) {\n        const currentPath = window.location.pathname.replace(/^\\/|\\/$/g,
    '');\n        document.getElementById('search').value = currentPath;\n    }\n\n//
    Search across all fields in an object\n    function searchObject(needle, obj)
    {\n        needle = needle.toLowerCase();\n        let score = 0;\n\n    // Helper
    to search a single field\n        const searchField = (value) => {\n            if
    (!value) return 0;\n            value = String(value).toLowerCase();\n\n            //
    Exact matches\n            if (value === needle) return 15;\n\n            //
    Word boundary matches (complete words)\n            if (value.match(new RegExp(`\\\\b${needle}\\\\b`)))
    return 10;\n\n            // Contains full search term\n            if (value.includes(needle))
    return 8;\n\n            // Most parts match (for multi-word searches)\n            const
    needleParts = needle.split(/\\W+/).filter(p => p.length > 2);\n            const
    valueParts = value.split(/\\W+/).filter(p => p.length > 2);\n\n            if
    (needleParts.length === 0) return 0;\n\n            let matchCount = 0;\n            for
    (const part of needleParts) {\n                for (const valuePart of valueParts)
    {\n                    if (valuePart.includes(part) || part.includes(valuePart))
    {\n                        matchCount++;\n                        break;\n                    }\n
    \               }\n            }\n\n            // Only count if most parts match\n
    \           const matchRatio = matchCount / needleParts.length;\n            if
    (matchRatio >= 0.75) {\n                return matchRatio * 6;\n            }\n\n
    \           return 0;\n        };\n\n    // Search each field with different weights\n
    \       const slugScore = searchField(obj.slug) * 3;  // Slug is most important\n
    \       const titleScore = searchField(obj.title) * 2;  // Title is next\n        const
    descScore = searchField(obj.description) * 1;  // Description\n        const tagScore
    = (obj.tags || []).reduce((sum, tag) => sum + searchField(tag), 0);  // Tags\n\n
    \       score = slugScore + titleScore + descScore + tagScore;\n\n    // Path
    segment matches for slug (only if we have some other match)\n        if (score
    > 0 && obj.slug) {\n            const inputParts = needle.split('/').filter(p
    => p.length > 0);\n            const slugParts = obj.slug.toLowerCase().split('/');\n\n
    \           // Bonus for matching path structure\n            for (let i = 0;
    i < inputParts.length && i < slugParts.length; i++) {\n                if (slugParts[i].includes(inputParts[i]))
    {\n                    score += 5;  // Matching segments in order is valuable\n
    \               }\n            }\n        }\n\n        return score;\n    }\n\n//
    Find similar pages\n    function findSimilar(input) {\n        if (!input || input.length
    < 2) return [];\n        const normalizedInput = input.toLowerCase().trim();\n\n
    \   // Score each page\n        const scored = pages.map(page => ({\n            ...page,\n
    \           score: searchObject(normalizedInput, page)\n        }));\n\n    //
    Sort by score (higher is better) and take top matches\n        return scored\n
    \           .sort((a, b) => b.score - a.score)\n            .slice(0, 12)  //
    Show more results in the grid\n            .filter(item => item.score > 15); //
    Only show strong matches\n    }\n\n// Update results in the DOM\n    function
    updateResults(results) {\n        const resultsDiv = document.getElementById('didyoumean_results');\n\n
    \       if (results.length === 0) {\n            resultsDiv.innerHTML = '<p class=\"text-gray-500
    col-span-full text-center py-8\">No similar pages found.</p>';\n            return;\n
    \       }\n\n        const html = results.map(page => `\n        <li class=\"p-4
    bg-gray-50 dark:bg-gray-800 rounded-lg hover:shadow-lg transition-shadow first:mt-4\">\n
    \           <a href=\"/${page.slug}\" class=\"block\">\n                <h3 class=\"text-lg
    font-semibold text-pink-500 hover:text-pink-600 dark:text-pink-400 dark:hover:text-pink-300
    mb-2\">\n                    ${page.title || page.slug}\n                </h3>\n

    dark:text-gray-300 mb-3 line-clamp-2\">\n            ${page.description}\n            </p>\n
    \           ` : ''}\n                <div class=\"flex flex-wrap gap-2 text-xs

    && page.tags.length > 0 ? `\n            <div class=\"mt-3 flex flex-wrap gap-2\">\n
    \           ${page.tags.map(tag => `\n                            <span class=\"px-2
    py-1 bg-gray-100 dark:bg-gray-700 rounded text-xs\">\n                                ${tag}\n
    \                           </span>\n                        `).join('')}\n            </div>\n
    \           ` : ''}\n            </a>\n        </li>\n    `).join('');\n\n        resultsDiv.innerHTML
    = html;\n    }\n\n// Set up hotkey for search if configured\n    if (search_hotkey)
    {\n        document.addEventListener('keydown', (e) => {\n            // Don't
    trigger if user is typing in an input or textarea\n            if (e.target.tagName
    === 'INPUT' || e.target.tagName === 'TEXTAREA') {\n                return;\n            }\n\n
    \           // Check if the pressed key matches the hotkey\n            if (e.key
    === search_hotkey) {\n                e.preventDefault();  // Prevent the '/'
    from being typed\n                const searchInput = document.getElementById('search');\n
    \               searchInput.focus();\n                searchInput.select();  //
    Select any existing text\n            }\n        });\n    }\n\n// Set up search

    document.getElementById('search');\n    searchInput.addEventListener('input',

    => {\n            const results = findSimilar(e.target.value);\n            updateResults(results);\n

    1.563 1 1 0 0 0 1.602-1.198z\"></path></svg></a></h2>\n<p>Okay, this is the oration
    for a blog post, maybe an open letter of sorts, as I am just wrestling with my
    new job and the responsibilities that either are put on me or that I've put on
    myself as the world is changing.\nAnd software development and software engineering
    and code writing\nis just changing, thanks to AI, thanks to agents and their introduction
    into the corporate environment.\nThe internet is full of opinions and ideas that
    push and pull and prod people in different directions.\nAnd every one of those
    ideas has contexts, which is necessary to keep in mind when filtering the ideas.\nAt
    a smaller startup where you can have a $200 a month Claude Code subscription for
    all of your engineers, that's gonna\nyield certain results that's going to encourage
    and invite certain people into that space to use those tools, the clawed tools,
    or Gemini or Codex.\nI work at a big company, Caterpillar.\nWe have a GitHub co-pilot,
    but through co-pilot we have access to the kind of frontier large language models.\nFrom
    there, we have access to the co-pilot harness, whether it's co-pilot CLI or the
    co-pilot integration with Visual Studio code, or the other IDEs like JetBrains,
    et cetera.\nOut there in the world, he has certain opinions from some people.\nYou
    have people who are very pro agents like Steve Yeage, who has been a big influence
    to me over the last several months, as I've definitely jumped into the deep end
    of agentic coding.\nI haven't even really opened my IDD in months, except for
    small things that I want to do by hand.\nenterprise grade work for the last three
    months or so and maybe a little longer has been fully written and produced by
    agents and i've been using AI to write code to some capacity or another since
    chad gpt came out really since like claude sonnet 3.5 but this thinking that i
    am doing is\nIt's really supposed to be about me, so this blog post I want to
    write is me processing thoughts on the expectation change that's placed on developers
    in certain environments for certain types of work.\nI guess even that, like that
    sounds kind of vague.\nSo I can only speak to myself.\nSo I need to put a big
    annotation and plaster this blog post with this big caveat message that this is
    me, this is my experience, this is my position, and this is not a truth that is
    wide reaching.\nIt's not any kind of prediction on the, you know,\nthe larger
    change happening in the world.\nThis is just where I am at.\nSo maybe for the
    post to make sense, I need to give a brief amount of history about me and my work.\nI'm
    not a trained software engineer, but I've been, I guess I've been a pretty impressive
    asset in my employment.\nI've been at Caterpillar for nine years, and in that
    nine years or over in the last several years, I've moonlit, moonlighted a little
    bit.\nAnd at every place I've done any other amount of work, I've just consistently
    impressed people.\nNow, this really just leads me to have more imposter syndrome
    because I know or I have learned what impresses people,\nAnd it isn't necessarily
    excellence, although I try to do things well and I want to solve problems and
    maintain the systems that I build to solve those problems.\nBut I don't write
    the best code.\nI'm not the smartest developer.\nI don't know the most.\nI get
    to know the things I work with and I learn a lot and I work fast because I have\nNot
    a super unique, but a rare ability to focus in, when the stars align, to focus
    and produce more than the next person.\nNot every next person, but a lot of the
    next people, a lot of the people I work with, I outperform.\nAnd that's been true,
    or it has seemed true, from every review, pretty much every review I've ever received,
    that's been true.\nAnd as I've had that just personal truth be reflected in my
    work, it's been enhanced with AI because I've been an early adopter and even as
    a, even as somewhat of a skeptic, like I've tried to maintain a posture of skepticism.\nBut
    since Opus 4.5,\nEven though I might talk like a skeptic, I am leaning into agents
    hard.\nAnd I am practicing my own harness engineering with my nexus project.\nAnd
    I'm using it to do engineering work at Caterpillar that's going into production
    for us, which is like data platform type of work.\nIt's not custom, it's not external
    customer facing.\nIt's internal tooling, which is what I mostly built.\nAnd the
    thing I'm really trying to wrap my head around, or I guess I'm trying to explain,
    is without AI, I was out producing a lot of people.\nAnd with AI, you can produce
    just a shit ton of code.\nAnd I'm trying to do that part well, but in my own gifts
    and curses of hyper focus and\nWanting to meet expectations and solve problems,
    I'm jumping into the new frontier very quickly.\nBut something I've noticed is
    that as I've produced a lot, people are just impressed.\nAnd they're not necessarily
    impressed with the excellence.\nThey're impressed with how I talk about things.\nThey're
    impressed with how much I do, and they're impressed with the output they see.\nBut
    the result of a bunch of code isn't all of the code itself.\nAnd I haven't read
    all the code I've produced with agents.\nI've certainly tried to test most of
    it or had most of it tested.\nBut as I've sought to do this and as I've been working
    in this capacity of using agents very heavily, I just feel kind of torn.\nin trusting
    the agents and in working with the agents in the ways that I think are appropriate,
    but also collaborating with other humans who are also using AI.\nBut are they
    using AI in a trustworthy way?\nI don't know, and I'm not sure how to validate
    it.\nBut my managers seem to think I know how to validate it, and they seem impressed
    with the work I do, and they seem impressed with my understanding of AI and agentic
    coding.\nAnd so I feel all of this pressure to continue to be a top performer
    like I have been since before AI.\nI feel a lot of pressure to, I don't want to
    coach people in how I do things, but I'm being asked to coach people and how I
    do things because other people I work with notice me.\nAnd I guess I'm almost,
    I'm just kind of tired of being noticed.\nBecause being noticed is not resulting
    in me becoming rich.\nAnd money is not the most important thing.\nBut man, I'd
    love to just make enough money to fuck off for the rest of my life and never look
    at a computer ever again.\nI would love...\nto not have to continue to maintain
    systems that I build or that other people build or to collaborate with people
    I don't know, who might not even be long term.\nThat's the one thing about Caterpillar
    is I'm working with a lot of contractors.\nAnd nothing wrong with contracting,
    I've done that.\nNothing wrong with being a contractor, but\nThey could disappear
    at any moment, and all the code that they write, and even today, all the code
    that they prompt and have an agent right becomes my problem, my maintenance issue,
    my responsibility.\nAnd it just is,\nIt's just making me nervous to maintain and
    to understand and to continue to work under conditions that I can't even tell
    if I'm making them for myself or if they're real expectations.\nLike if I fell
    off the face of the earth or if I dropped agentic coding entirely and I just went
    back to coding by hand completely, would my output continue to impress people
    today?\nI don't know the answer to that.\nBut I certainly wouldn't be solving
    problems as fast, but would I be creating less problems?\nLike I've solved a lot
    of problems in three months in my new role here with Forge.\nBut I don't know
    how many problems I've created.\nOnly time will tell.\nAnd the thing is, the guys
    I work with who are contractors, they might not be around long enough for time
    to tell, but because I'm not rich yet, I probably will be long enough, or be around
    long enough.\nto experience those bugs and those problems that I don't know about
    yet.\nAnd I am just feeling so much pressure to be the best and to be the one
    to solve problems.\nAnd I don't know if I'm putting it on me or if cat's putting
    it on me or if my project manager is putting it on me.\nI'm just really struggling
    with the amount of responsibility I feel.\nAnd I'm feeling frustrated about it
    because that amount of responsibility feels like it has scaled a lot with agents,
    with AI, but not my paycheck.\nAnd in fact, my paycheck has gone down quite a
    bit.\nAnd I'm not more relaxed.\nI'm making about half of what I've made over
    the last couple of years.\nAnd I'm not more relaxed.\nI don't feel freer.\nI don't
    feel like I have 50% of the responsibility that I did.\nCal Autonomy awoke something
    in me for purpose, but that feels like it's already starting to die.\nI'm already
    tired.\nI'm, I don't know about burnt out, but I'm tired.\nI was reviewing some
    code that one of these contractors I'm working with.\nIt's actually only an intern.\nHe's
    not even a contractor.\nHe's an intern.\nAnd I'm reviewing a pull request of his.\nAnd
    damn, it's a lot of code.\nAnd it looks somewhat useful.\nBut I don't know exactly
    how to test it.\nThat's not true actually, I do know how to test it, but I don't
    own enough of our infrastructure at Caterpillar to test it easily.\nSo do I trust
    him?\nDo I trust his prompt?\nDo I trust his harness?\nDo I trust the tests that
    I have already put in the code base to capture and block any breakages he might
    have introduced with this code for the code I've already been producing?\nIt's
    different when I'm intimately familiar with the code base.\nAnd I've been, I don't
    know, definitely two other places at least where I had very intimate familiarity
    with the system and with the code and with the platform.\nBut the one I'm on right
    now is new, and I built it.\nI built it with agents.\nI built it to replace a
    thing that was never even working.\nBut I built it.\nIt's mine.\nIt's not somebody
    else's.\nIt's not that we'll document it.\nThere's a lot of documentation, but
    it's all been agent produced, and even though I've reviewed it, it's hard for
    me to organize it in the right way in a way that makes sense.\nI'm just feeling
    so frustrated that it seems like, as the tools are getting better, as developers
    are able to move faster, it feels like I am expected to move faster.\nbut I'm
    not expected to make any more money.\nI'm not expected to be compensated anymore
    for the increase in velocity and responsibility.\nIt's almost like if I could
    produce, this metric is not gonna be real, but let's just, for the sake of argument,
    make it up.\nIf I can write 30,000 lines of code in a year and make $100,000,
    I feel fine about that, whatever that number means.\nBut now I can actually produce
    300,000 lines of code in a year.\nI'm definitely producing 10 times more code
    than normal.\nI don't know if I'm doing 10 times more engineering, but I am making
    exactly the same amount of money.\nAnd so why did I do it?\nWhy am I leaning into
    it so hard?\nWhy am I trying to still be the front runner to be on the bleeding
    edge when caterpillar is not going to pay anymore?\nAnd I could go somewhere else.\nI
    could go to Avant.\nI could make more.\nIt's a system I'm familiar with.\nIt's
    even people I know.\nAnd there's AI there too.\nBut it's a system I know.\nIt's
    a system I know how to support and maintain.\nAnd there's not an expectation that
    I'm going to produce 10,000 lines of code this week.\nBut right now at Caterpillar,
    it feels like I am expected to write 10,000 lines of code this week.\nAnd deploy
    it and maintain it and solve new problems.\nAll in an incredibly political and
    hectic, chaotic environment where there isn't an appropriate amount of leadership
    over me.\nI am the expert in AI in my group.\nAnd that feels retarded.\nI am the
    professional agentic engineer and that is just scary because I'm not good enough
    to be that.\nI'm not Steve Yeagie, I'm not Simon Willison, I haven't been a developer
    for 20 years.\nI'm just a guy who learned some Python to solve some problems and
    happen to work faster than a lot of people and that reputation has really\nIt's
    treated me well for sure.\nAnd my disposition to problem solving has, up until
    recently, I felt like I had been, like I've been compensated well by my company,
    but then I've also been able to do more work outside of one job for me to feel
    the value of my own skills.\nBut now with AI changing everything,\nCan I show
    you what's on the back of my story?\nIt feels like I've given up the personal
    gains.\nOne second, Alia.\nSo I've given up personally realizing the benefit of
    my own skill.\nAnd now I am like in a position where\nCaterpillar, and only Caterpillar,
    is reaping the benefit of my new scheme in agentic engineering.\nAnd I think that's
    just ticking me off because I stayed with Catt to do something good for the world.\nAnd
    I think we can do something good for the world.\nBut I'm tired.</p>\n<h2 id=\"notes-and-summarization\">Notes

    be a personal essay, not a manifesto about AI. The core tension:</p>\n<ul>\n<li>agents
    made me dramatically more productive</li>\n<li>that productivity increased the
    responsibility I carry</li>\n<li>the financial upside I used to realize personally
    has gone down, not up</li>\n</ul>\n<h2 id=\"section-stub-opening-scene\">Section

    with a concrete moment. Maybe reviewing a massive PR from the intern / contractor
    side of the system.</li>\n<li>What did I feel in that moment: confusion, dread,
    pressure, responsibility, skepticism?</li>\n<li>What was the specific thought:
    &quot;I might own all of this later, even if I did not really produce or validate
    all of it myself.&quot;</li>\n<li>Can I open with one sharp sentence that names
    the tension right away?</li>\n</ul>\n<p>Possible opener idea:</p>\n<p>&quot;Agentic
    coding has made me faster than ever, but it has also made me feel responsible
    for more than I can honestly hold in my head.&quot;</p>\n<h3>My Opener</h3>\n<p>Agentic
    coding has been an exciting change to me over the last couple of months\nspecifically.
    I've been using AI tools for a few years now but something really\nshifted with
    Opus 4.5 as well as the tools/harnesses getting better and more\nuseful around
    the same time. I've been influenced by people like Simon Willison\nand Steve Yegge
    who have been on the forefront of agentic coding and <a href=\"https://simonwillison.net/2025/Oct/7/vibe-engineering/\">vibe\nengineering</a>
    since the\ndawn of ChatGPT and in my small circles of work I'm definitely on the
    bloodiest\nbleeding edge of the adoption of these practices. Are they the future?
    I don't\nknow - I tried to maintain a skeptical posture but until the bubble pops
    it's\nlooking like this is at least a direction the future of my line of work
    is\ngoing.</p>\n<p>The tough part is mixing the new world of agentic coding with\ndevelopers
    on wildly different points in the spectrum of adoption and maturity.\nHere's a
    short anecdote about what I mean...</p>\n<p>In a new project that I'm on, I've
    refactored/reimplemented a lot of legacy\ncode, producing about 140k lines of
    code, configuration, and\ndocs in about 2 months. It's an insane amount of &quot;product&quot;
    and I've done it\nentirely with agents. But I didn't do it with a handful of vanilla
    chat\nsessions like &quot;Hey Copilot, reimplement this API, no mistakes&quot;.
    I've been building\nout my own process for using LLMs effectively. I have no actual
    idea besides my\nown experience if what I'm building is useful, but it feels pretty
    good -\nagents for planning, building, reviewing, testing, etc. My &quot;harness&quot;
    includes\nspecialized agents and opinionated development workflows\nto try to
    ensure that code is never one-shotted into production.</p>\n<p>The catch though
    is that I'm working with a handful of developers and they are not\nearly-adopters
    or aggressive experimenters with these new agentic tools. Most\nof them are still
    in Steve Yegge's stage 2 or 3 of AI coding as he's outlined\n<a href=\"https://steve-yegge.medium.com/welcome-to-gas-town-4f25ee16dd04\">in
    this medium\narticle introducing\nGasTown</a>. They\nopen a chat session, and
    say yes or no to . I've been in-between stages\n6 and 7 for a while now - managing
    multiple agentic sessions that themselves\nrun specialized subagents primiarly
    for context management, although I don't\nfeel quite ready for full blown stage
    8 (agents running agnets running agents).\nSo this isn't me saying that I'm\nbetter
    than the guys I'm working with or anything, just the project we're on\ntogether
    is now being touched by people on wildly different ends of the agentic\ncoding
    spectrum.</p>\n<p>Finally the anecdote - I'm getting PRs from guys in stages 2
    and 3,\nthousands of lines of code and config, that I know they are not necessarily\nexperts
    in, but they're producing that code with fairly vanilla practices. How\ndo I verify
    it? I know my own prompts, I know what agents generated my code, I know\nthe direction
    and prodding I gave as they're building, but all\nI know in a PR review from someone
    else is the diff - not how they tested it\n(unless of course there's tests, and
    there must be, but tests are only as good\nas the tests are...), not the steering
    they gave, not the manual UAT validation\nthey did... And ultimately I own the
    code they merge because chances are I\noutlast their contract with the team. It's
    kind of a scary thing to review and\naccept... do I trust my\nagentic practices
    to validate their work? I genuinely don't know, and I also\nliterally can't sleep
    at night.</p>\n<h2 id=\"section-stub-this-is-about-me-not-everyone\">Section Stub:

    a strong caveat early: this is not prediction, prescription, or universal truth.</li>\n<li>Name
    my context: big company, internal platform work, political environment, mixed
    team ownership, frontier models via Copilot.</li>\n<li>What about my temperament
    matters here? Hyperfocus, initiative, problem solving, pressure sensitivity, desire
    to meet expectations.</li>\n<li>What would I want a reader to not mishear?</li>\n</ul>\n<h3>My
    section about me</h3>\n<p>I want to make sure to reiterate the context of this
    post and my thoughts -\nit's really about me. I'm not saying anything here is
    true for anyone else, I'm\nnot making predictions about tomorrow, and I'm not
    a prominent FOSS developer or a\nhigh-profile ex-FAANG engineer. I work at a big
    company, not a fast-paced\nstartup. I work on internal tooling, nothing that should
    even be seen outside\nour network. I'm also a self-taught developer, not a trained
    software engineer.</p>\n<p>As far as AI goes, lately I'm mostly all in on Github
    Copilot\nCLI with a sprinkling of Opencode. I'm not using Claude Code or Codex
    which\nhave seem to have their own communities around plugins and usage.</p>\n<p>And
    since we're so focused on me right now, the important thing to keep as the\nbackdrop
    for this post is my temperament and make-up. I'm definitely made a\ncertain kind
    of way, not different than every single person necessarily, but\ndifferent enough,
    from enough people, to not blend in with the 9-5 guy who can\nlog out and not
    think about work until the morning (I'm not saying that's a\nstrength either,
    my boundary problems are for another time).</p>\n<p>I feel an aggressive burden
    to solve problems and own those solutions, call it\nwhite-knighting or a savior
    complex if you want, but I've got enough of a\nreputation at Caterpillar now (and
    anywhere else I've worked) to become a go-to\nperson for more than I think is
    necessarily appropriate. And with that burden\nI'll bring results - if a problem
    hooks into my brain it. will. be. solved. I\nprobably won't do the best most clean-code
    solution right out of the gate, but\nI'll do whatever I can to solve it.</p>\n<h2

    is who I was before agents made code easy to produce. My first boss at\nCaterpillar
    used the phrase &quot;tenacious learner&quot; to describe me in several\nreviews.
    I kind of rejected the description because I basically refused to\nbelieve that
    I was really any different, any harder of a worker, than my peers.\nBut with almost
    a decade of experience in the corporate world, and some adult\nperspective on
    my life, I think it's accurate... I am a harder worker, to my\ndetriment sometimes
    and I need to put up some firmer boundaries. I'm not\nbrilliant but I can focus
    for a long time in the right circumstances. The gift\nof perseverence (or the
    curse of not being able to let something go, depending\non how you look at it)
    has led to blessing in my life in both reward and skill.</p>\n<p>And then AI came
    along and with another set of the right circumstances\ncatalyzed a new way to
    work.</p>\n<h2 id=\"section-stub-why-i-leaned-into-agentic-coding\">Section Stub:

    jumped onto experimenting with AI coding tools as soon as they became\navailable,
    but mostly just tried vibe coding rather than using tools for real\nengineering
    work. I vibed up an API at one of my jobs that went into production\nway too early,
    with far too little validation, and it was scary to support it\nfrom then on out.
    That was before\nagents were quite as useful as they can be now, but that experience
    along with\na handful of other stepping stones began to give me confidence in
    using the AI\nas a tool, like my IDE is a tool, for producing <strong>solutions</strong>
    that take the form of\ncode.</p>\n<p>As one-shot apps got better and better, and
    as I learned about scoping work\nmore appropriately for agentic tools my confidence
    in them grew. There's quite\na difference between &quot;Claude make me a todo
    app, no mistakes&quot; and scoping out a\nsolution in natural language, with some
    technical guardrails, and having agents\ntackle the implementation methodically.</p>\n<h2
    id=\"section-stub-what-actually-changed\">Section Stub: What Actually Changed

    the practical change in my day-to-day work.</li>\n<li>I am no longer mainly writing
    code line by line; I am directing, reviewing, testing, and integrating generated
    work.</li>\n<li>What changed about the quantity of output?</li>\n<li>What changed
    about how coworkers and managers perceived me?</li>\n<li>What changed about the
    kind of questions people started asking me?</li>\n</ul>\n<p>Questions to answer:</p>\n<ul>\n<li>When
    did I realize this was not just autocomplete, but a new mode of working?</li>\n<li>What
    does &quot;enterprise-grade work fully written and produced by agents&quot; actually
    mean in practice?</li>\n</ul>\n<h3>My section on what actually changed</h3>\n<p>What's
    actually changed for me is quite a lot... I haven't opened my IDE to\nseriously
    write code for months now. I've oscillated between Opencode and\nCopilot CLI,
    leaning moreso into Copilot since it's an approved tool at work\nand as of mid-February
    is quite good. Mentally I'm approaching problems with a\nlittle more thought on
    the front-end than before because prior to agents I\nwould think as I implemented.
    At the scale of work that I do, this was really\nfine - working on CLI utilities
    to solve simple problems, developing an\niterative testing cycle for each problem
    that allowed me to move fast, and once\nI found a groove I was cooking. But now
    I don't even need to find it, I open\nOpencode or Copilot CLI with my Planner
    agent, describe what I want to happen and have\nOpus or GPT scope out a plan for
    me. Usually there's some back and forth on\nfeature scoping, then I review a markdown
    file it produces, and once it looks\ndecent enough to me I say &quot;go&quot;
    and it goes.</p>\n<p>That works a lot better than I even care to admit because
    at the same time as\nI've been leaning harder into agents, I've been building
    my own harness of\nsorts - not a replacement for Copilot CLI or a competitor to
    Opencode, but\nmoreso an opinionated workflow spine that I force agents into,
    oh and I'm\ndefining the agents, to give strict gates to the SDLC (software development\nlifecycle).
    Plan and implement is fine for a lot of things, and I do think it's\nonly getting
    better. My harness, mentioned a few times around here before,\ncalled Nexus, is
    a set of agents and rules that I want the code I'll be\nresponsible for to go
    through before it lands in production. That cycle isn't\ntoo complex, and there's
    only about 10,000 similar tools to Nexus on Github\ntrending right now. I've thought
    about dropping my idea and picking up\nsomething more popular, like\n<a href=\"https://www.github.com/obra/superpowers\">superpowers</a>
    but at the moment I'm\ncontinuing to develop on and lean into my own idea here.
    I keep saying a blog\npost is coming, but the high level of Nexus is that it's
    a task\ntracker with a CLI that agents use to advance a ticket through a plan
    -&gt; build\n-&gt; test -&gt; review -&gt; verify -&gt; merge lifecycle that is
    almost exactly how I\nwould otherwise have solved a problem by hand. I think it
    needs work, I need to\nbe harder on TDD methodlogies with agents, and work on
    verification gating a\nbit more (shoutout to <a href=\"https://github.com/simonw/showboat\">showboat</a>
    by Simon\nWillison) but overall it's a system of thinking that I already participate
    in\nso I'm doing my best to farm out specific parts of my workflow to agents rather\nthan
    trying to one-shot enterprise problems and solutions.</p>\n<p>I've noticed that
    as I've developed Nexus out though, I lean on the agents for\nmore and more of
    my own thinking, and am trusting my problem solving\n<strong>process</strong>
    moreso than my actual problem solving abilities.</p>\n<h2 id=\"section-stub-the-hidden-cost-of-speed\">Section

    section on hidden cost</h3>\n<p>The cost of this increase in speed is a lack of
    familiarity - and the fallout\nof lack of familiarity is hard to express. There's
    also many facets to it.\nFor me, the first facet is that Nexus helps me move fast,
    but as I've leaned\ninto it for more and more of\nthe planning, I'm less and less
    familiar with the state of the code. I find\nmyself asking my reviewer agents
    in fresh sessions often to explain it to me,\nand thankfully they're usually consistent,
    but nonetheless I'm still not\nintimtaely familiar with the code. And on Nexus
    it's not a big deal, that's low stakes, it's just me and my workflow.</p>\n<p>I'm
    using Nexus + Copilot at work and that feels like higher\nstakes... I have my
    agents explain the status of our project and although they're\nalso somewhat consistent
    the thing that's scary is that other people are\nworking on that repo with me,
    and that's where another layer of complexity\nmanifests itself. If its' just me
    and my clankers, let's go all day long,\nrebuild, ask questions, etc... but I
    have other developers I rub shoulders with\nnow, and if they ask me a question
    what am I going to say? &quot;Hold on, let me prompt\nmy agent for you&quot; -
    it's LMGTFY on steroids. And the burden becomes if I\nfeel like I can own and
    support what those other developers push into the repo.</p>\n<h2 id=\"section-stub-responsibility-without-clear-boundaries\">Section

    become my long-term maintenance problem.</li>\n<li>The organization sees me as
    someone who understands AI and agents, so responsibility pools onto me.</li>\n<li>Ask
    whether the pressure is explicit or self-imposed, but note that the body feels

    accountable when AI-assisted code breaks six months from now?</li>\n<li>What does
    it mean to be treated like the local expert when I do not feel like one?</li>\n<li>Why
    is being noticed beginning to feel costly instead of rewarding?</li>\n</ul>\n<h3>My
    section on responsibility</h3>\n<p>Why do I own their work? Well for the third
    time, this is about me, and my\nsituation is that the other developers I work
    with presently are all contractors.\nTheir work agreement with Cat could end at
    any second, for practically any\nreason. That's technically true for me as well,
    seeing as how Cat is an\n&quot;at-will&quot; employer (they can fire anyone for
    no reason at any time). But the\nincentive structure isn't there for these guys
    who technically work for an\nagency... their bonuses aren't bigger (or even exist)
    if Cat performs well,\nthere's no extra vacation days in it for em (aye, contractors
    don't get\nvacation days anyways), and not that it's a personal problem - but
    they're\nbasically mercenaries out to the highest bidder and I happen to know
    of\n<strong>multiple times where Cat lost a good person to a higher bidder</strong>.</p>\n<p>So
    this isn't really me trying to be negative about contractors at all, I'm\nhere
    for a pay-check as well but Cat at least gives me SOME incentive to work\nhard
    with the goal of compensation regardless of how altruistic I feel in my\nown circumstances.</p>\n<p>It's
    also not all about the fact that the other guys aren't on Cat's primary\npayroll,
    I've dug myself quite a hole over the last 8-10 years, gaining a\nreputation that
    I think many would appreciate, but for me only increases the\nstress. I dont'
    need to parrot every accolade I've ever received, that's not\nthe point, but to
    make the point as clear as I can - I have a\nlot of respect from quite a few people
    at Caterpillar. I'm blessed to have that\nreputation, and it's not like I haven't
    worked hard for it - but people talk\nabout me in a way so flattering I feel like
    the main character in a fictional\nstory sometimes.</p>\n<p>In a fictional story
    I can check out the ending, hit up spark notes, or ask AI\nhow it ends... but
    there isn't an &quot;end&quot; in my real world scenario, there's only\ntomorrow
    and I feel the pressure of not knowing what tomorrow holds now more\nthan ever.</p>\n<p>Being
    noticed is starting to feel more costly than rewarding...</p>\n<h2 id=\"section-stub-the-financial-irony\">Section
