    section on hidden cost</h3>\n<p>The cost of this increase in speed is a lack of
    familiarity - and the fallout\nof lack of familiarity is hard to express. There's
    also many facets to it.\nFor me, the first facet is that Nexus helps me move fast,
    but as I've leaned\ninto it for more and more of\nthe planning, I'm less and less
    familiar with the state of the code. I find\nmyself asking my reviewer agents
    in fresh sessions often to explain it to me,\nand thankfully they're usually consistent,
    but nonetheless I'm still not\nintimtaely familiar with the code. And on Nexus
    it's not a big deal, that's low stakes, it's just me and my workflow.</p>\n<p>I'm
    using Nexus + Copilot at work and that feels like higher\nstakes... I have my
    agents explain the status of our project and although they're\nalso somewhat consistent
    the thing that's scary is that other people are\nworking on that repo with me,
    and that's where another layer of complexity\nmanifests itself. If its' just me
    and my clankers, let's go all day long,\nrebuild, ask questions, etc... but I
    have other developers I rub shoulders with\nnow, and if they ask me a question
    what am I going to say? &quot;Hold on, let me prompt\nmy agent for you&quot; -
    it's LMGTFY on steroids. And the burden becomes if I\nfeel like I can own and
    support what those other developers push into the repo.</p>\n<h2 id=\"section-stub-responsibility-without-clear-boundaries\">Section

    section on responsibility</h3>\n<p>Why do I own their work? Well for the third
    time, this is about me, and my\nsituation is that the other developers I work
    with presently are all contractors.\nTheir work agreement with Cat could end at
    any second, for practically any\nreason. That's technically true for me as well,
    seeing as how Cat is an\n&quot;at-will&quot; employer (they can fire anyone for
    no reason at any time). But the\nincentive structure isn't there for these guys
    who technically work for an\nagency... their bonuses aren't bigger (or even exist)
    if Cat performs well,\nthere's no extra vacation days in it for em (aye, contractors
    don't get\nvacation days anyways), and not that it's a personal problem - but
    they're\nbasically mercenaries out to the highest bidder and I happen to know
    of\n<strong>multiple times where Cat lost a good person to a higher bidder</strong>.</p>\n<p>So
    this isn't really me trying to be negative about contractors at all, I'm\nhere
    for a pay-check as well but Cat at least gives me SOME incentive to work\nhard
    with the goal of compensation regardless of how altruistic I feel in my\nown circumstances.</p>\n<p>It's
    also not all about the fact that the other guys aren't on Cat's primary\npayroll,
    I've dug myself quite a hole over the last 8-10 years, gaining a\nreputation that
    I think many would appreciate, but for me only increases the\nstress. I dont'
    need to parrot every accolade I've ever received, that's not\nthe point, but to
    make the point as clear as I can - I have a\nlot of respect from quite a few people
    at Caterpillar. I'm blessed to have that\nreputation, and it's not like I haven't
    worked hard for it - but people talk\nabout me in a way so flattering I feel like
    the main character in a fictional\nstory sometimes.</p>\n<p>In a fictional story
    I can check out the ending, hit up spark notes, or ask AI\nhow it ends... but
    there isn't an &quot;end&quot; in my real world scenario, there's only\ntomorrow
    and I feel the pressure of not knowing what tomorrow holds now more\nthan ever.</p>\n<p>Being
    noticed is starting to feel more costly than rewarding...</p>\n<h2 id=\"section-stub-the-financial-irony\">Section
    Stub: The Financial Irony <a class=\"header-anchor\" href=\"#section-stub-the-financial-irony\"><svg

    1.563 1 1 0 0 0 1.602-1.198z\"></path></svg></a></h2>\n<p>Prompt:</p>\n<ul>\n<li>State
    this clearly and concretely.</li>\n<li>For years, I realized more value from my
    output because I worked two jobs concurrently.</li>\n<li>Due to the new role,
    new responsibility, and the demands of agentic coding in this environment, I had
    to go down to one job.</li>\n<li>So the irony is: the tools make me dramatically
    more productive, but I personally realize less economic upside from my own gifts
    and skills than I did before.</li>\n<li>Keep this grounded and honest, not whiny.</li>\n</ul>\n<p>Questions
    to answer:</p>\n<ul>\n<li>What exactly have I lost financially?</li>\n<li>Why
    does this feel bigger than &quot;I want more money&quot;?</li>\n<li>Where do the
    gains of AI go when an individual contributor gets faster?</li>\n<li>If I can
    now produce 10x more code, why does my life not feel lighter, freer, or better
    compensated?</li>\n</ul>\n<p>Possible thesis sentence:</p>\n<p>&quot;Agentic coding
    increased the value I create at work, while decreasing the share of that value
    I personally get to realize.&quot;</p>\n<h3>My section on cost</h3>\n<p>What's
    the cost? It's hard to get specific without writing a novel but here's\nthe TLDR
    - because I've been pretty good at what I do I've been able to do this\ntype of
    work outside my normal 9-5 responsibilities and with that extra work\nhas been
    some pretty great financial benefits. However with Cat changes,\nresponsibility
    increases, and now owning code that others (and their clankers)\nwrite, the extra
    time I gained for myself is eaten-up and has been reclaimed by\nthe mega-corp...
    &quot;Exceeding expectations&quot; every year just meant the bar is\nraised, the
    expectations are higher, the time-commitment requirement is higher,\nand as I've
    had to meet the requirements of both the new world and the curse of\nbeing noticed,
    I've lost the time for the extra work... For years I've realized\nthe benefit
    of my own skills and drive, but the irony of agents (and a handful\nof other things)
    is that with the dramatic increase in expectations, not only\non me but on those
    I work with and therefore their output, I don't get to\nrealize the benefits of
    my own gifts anymore.</p>\n<h2 id=\"section-stub-mission-meaning-and-fatigue\">Section

  raw.md: "---\ndate: 2026-03-16 20:18:23\ntemplateKey: note\ntitle: Oration on Agents
    and being crushed by responsibility\npublished: False\ntags:\n  - ai\n  - note\n---\n\nThe
    intention of this note is to become a blog post:\n\n## Raw Recording\n\nOkay,
    this is the oration for a blog post, maybe an open letter of sorts, as I am just
    wrestling with my new job and the responsibilities that either are put on me or
    that I've put on myself as the world is changing.\nAnd software development and
    software engineering and code writing\nis just changing, thanks to AI, thanks
    to agents and their introduction into the corporate environment.\nThe internet
    is full of opinions and ideas that push and pull and prod people in different
    directions.\nAnd every one of those ideas has contexts, which is necessary to
    keep in mind when filtering the ideas.\nAt a smaller startup where you can have
    a $200 a month Claude Code subscription for all of your engineers, that's gonna\nyield
    certain results that's going to encourage and invite certain people into that
    space to use those tools, the clawed tools, or Gemini or Codex.\nI work at a big
    company, Caterpillar.\nWe have a GitHub co-pilot, but through co-pilot we have
    access to the kind of frontier large language models.\nFrom there, we have access
    to the co-pilot harness, whether it's co-pilot CLI or the co-pilot integration
    with Visual Studio code, or the other IDEs like JetBrains, et cetera.\nOut there
    in the world, he has certain opinions from some people.\nYou have people who are
    very pro agents like Steve Yeage, who has been a big influence to me over the
    last several months, as I've definitely jumped into the deep end of agentic coding.\nI
    haven't even really opened my IDD in months, except for small things that I want
    to do by hand.\nenterprise grade work for the last three months or so and maybe
    a little longer has been fully written and produced by agents and i've been using
    AI to write code to some capacity or another since chad gpt came out really since
    like claude sonnet 3.5 but this thinking that i am doing is\nIt's really supposed
    to be about me, so this blog post I want to write is me processing thoughts on
    the expectation change that's placed on developers in certain environments for
    certain types of work.\nI guess even that, like that sounds kind of vague.\nSo
    I can only speak to myself.\nSo I need to put a big annotation and plaster this
    blog post with this big caveat message that this is me, this is my experience,
    this is my position, and this is not a truth that is wide reaching.\nIt's not
    any kind of prediction on the, you know,\nthe larger change happening in the world.\nThis
    is just where I am at.\nSo maybe for the post to make sense, I need to give a
    brief amount of history about me and my work.\nI'm not a trained software engineer,
    but I've been, I guess I've been a pretty impressive asset in my employment.\nI've
    been at Caterpillar for nine years, and in that nine years or over in the last
    several years, I've moonlit, moonlighted a little bit.\nAnd at every place I've
    done any other amount of work, I've just consistently impressed people.\nNow,
    this really just leads me to have more imposter syndrome because I know or I have
    learned what impresses people,\nAnd it isn't necessarily excellence, although
    I try to do things well and I want to solve problems and maintain the systems
    that I build to solve those problems.\nBut I don't write the best code.\nI'm not
    the smartest developer.\nI don't know the most.\nI get to know the things I work
    with and I learn a lot and I work fast because I have\nNot a super unique, but
    a rare ability to focus in, when the stars align, to focus and produce more than
    the next person.\nNot every next person, but a lot of the next people, a lot of
    the people I work with, I outperform.\nAnd that's been true, or it has seemed
    true, from every review, pretty much every review I've ever received, that's been
    true.\nAnd as I've had that just personal truth be reflected in my work, it's
    been enhanced with AI because I've been an early adopter and even as a, even as
    somewhat of a skeptic, like I've tried to maintain a posture of skepticism.\nBut
    since Opus 4.5,\nEven though I might talk like a skeptic, I am leaning into agents
    hard.\nAnd I am practicing my own harness engineering with my nexus project.\nAnd
    I'm using it to do engineering work at Caterpillar that's going into production
    for us, which is like data platform type of work.\nIt's not custom, it's not external
    customer facing.\nIt's internal tooling, which is what I mostly built.\nAnd the
    thing I'm really trying to wrap my head around, or I guess I'm trying to explain,
    is without AI, I was out producing a lot of people.\nAnd with AI, you can produce
    just a shit ton of code.\nAnd I'm trying to do that part well, but in my own gifts
    and curses of hyper focus and\nWanting to meet expectations and solve problems,
    I'm jumping into the new frontier very quickly.\nBut something I've noticed is
    that as I've produced a lot, people are just impressed.\nAnd they're not necessarily
    impressed with the excellence.\nThey're impressed with how I talk about things.\nThey're
    impressed with how much I do, and they're impressed with the output they see.\nBut
    the result of a bunch of code isn't all of the code itself.\nAnd I haven't read
    all the code I've produced with agents.\nI've certainly tried to test most of
    it or had most of it tested.\nBut as I've sought to do this and as I've been working
    in this capacity of using agents very heavily, I just feel kind of torn.\nin trusting
    the agents and in working with the agents in the ways that I think are appropriate,
    but also collaborating with other humans who are also using AI.\nBut are they
    using AI in a trustworthy way?\nI don't know, and I'm not sure how to validate
    it.\nBut my managers seem to think I know how to validate it, and they seem impressed
    with the work I do, and they seem impressed with my understanding of AI and agentic
    coding.\nAnd so I feel all of this pressure to continue to be a top performer
    like I have been since before AI.\nI feel a lot of pressure to, I don't want to
    coach people in how I do things, but I'm being asked to coach people and how I
    do things because other people I work with notice me.\nAnd I guess I'm almost,
    I'm just kind of tired of being noticed.\nBecause being noticed is not resulting
    in me becoming rich.\nAnd money is not the most important thing.\nBut man, I'd
    love to just make enough money to fuck off for the rest of my life and never look
    at a computer ever again.\nI would love...\nto not have to continue to maintain
    systems that I build or that other people build or to collaborate with people
    I don't know, who might not even be long term.\nThat's the one thing about Caterpillar
    is I'm working with a lot of contractors.\nAnd nothing wrong with contracting,
    I've done that.\nNothing wrong with being a contractor, but\nThey could disappear
    at any moment, and all the code that they write, and even today, all the code
    that they prompt and have an agent right becomes my problem, my maintenance issue,
    my responsibility.\nAnd it just is,\nIt's just making me nervous to maintain and
    to understand and to continue to work under conditions that I can't even tell
    if I'm making them for myself or if they're real expectations.\nLike if I fell
    off the face of the earth or if I dropped agentic coding entirely and I just went
    back to coding by hand completely, would my output continue to impress people
    today?\nI don't know the answer to that.\nBut I certainly wouldn't be solving
    problems as fast, but would I be creating less problems?\nLike I've solved a lot
    of problems in three months in my new role here with Forge.\nBut I don't know
    how many problems I've created.\nOnly time will tell.\nAnd the thing is, the guys
    I work with who are contractors, they might not be around long enough for time
    to tell, but because I'm not rich yet, I probably will be long enough, or be around
    long enough.\nto experience those bugs and those problems that I don't know about
    yet.\nAnd I am just feeling so much pressure to be the best and to be the one
    to solve problems.\nAnd I don't know if I'm putting it on me or if cat's putting
    it on me or if my project manager is putting it on me.\nI'm just really struggling
    with the amount of responsibility I feel.\nAnd I'm feeling frustrated about it
    because that amount of responsibility feels like it has scaled a lot with agents,
    with AI, but not my paycheck.\nAnd in fact, my paycheck has gone down quite a
    bit.\nAnd I'm not more relaxed.\nI'm making about half of what I've made over
    the last couple of years.\nAnd I'm not more relaxed.\nI don't feel freer.\nI don't
    feel like I have 50% of the responsibility that I did.\nCal Autonomy awoke something
    in me for purpose, but that feels like it's already starting to die.\nI'm already
    tired.\nI'm, I don't know about burnt out, but I'm tired.\nI was reviewing some
    code that one of these contractors I'm working with.\nIt's actually only an intern.\nHe's
    not even a contractor.\nHe's an intern.\nAnd I'm reviewing a pull request of his.\nAnd
    damn, it's a lot of code.\nAnd it looks somewhat useful.\nBut I don't know exactly
    how to test it.\nThat's not true actually, I do know how to test it, but I don't
    own enough of our infrastructure at Caterpillar to test it easily.\nSo do I trust
    him?\nDo I trust his prompt?\nDo I trust his harness?\nDo I trust the tests that
    I have already put in the code base to capture and block any breakages he might
    have introduced with this code for the code I've already been producing?\nIt's
    different when I'm intimately familiar with the code base.\nAnd I've been, I don't
    know, definitely two other places at least where I had very intimate familiarity
    with the system and with the code and with the platform.\nBut the one I'm on right
    now is new, and I built it.\nI built it with agents.\nI built it to replace a
    thing that was never even working.\nBut I built it.\nIt's mine.\nIt's not somebody
    else's.\nIt's not that we'll document it.\nThere's a lot of documentation, but
    it's all been agent produced, and even though I've reviewed it, it's hard for
    me to organize it in the right way in a way that makes sense.\nI'm just feeling
    so frustrated that it seems like, as the tools are getting better, as developers
    are able to move faster, it feels like I am expected to move faster.\nbut I'm
    not expected to make any more money.\nI'm not expected to be compensated anymore
    for the increase in velocity and responsibility.\nIt's almost like if I could
    produce, this metric is not gonna be real, but let's just, for the sake of argument,
    make it up.\nIf I can write 30,000 lines of code in a year and make $100,000,
    I feel fine about that, whatever that number means.\nBut now I can actually produce
    300,000 lines of code in a year.\nI'm definitely producing 10 times more code
    than normal.\nI don't know if I'm doing 10 times more engineering, but I am making
    exactly the same amount of money.\nAnd so why did I do it?\nWhy am I leaning into
    it so hard?\nWhy am I trying to still be the front runner to be on the bleeding
    edge when caterpillar is not going to pay anymore?\nAnd I could go somewhere else.\nI
    could go to Avant.\nI could make more.\nIt's a system I'm familiar with.\nIt's
    even people I know.\nAnd there's AI there too.\nBut it's a system I know.\nIt's
    a system I know how to support and maintain.\nAnd there's not an expectation that
    I'm going to produce 10,000 lines of code this week.\nBut right now at Caterpillar,
    it feels like I am expected to write 10,000 lines of code this week.\nAnd deploy
    it and maintain it and solve new problems.\nAll in an incredibly political and
    hectic, chaotic environment where there isn't an appropriate amount of leadership
    over me.\nI am the expert in AI in my group.\nAnd that feels retarded.\nI am the
    professional agentic engineer and that is just scary because I'm not good enough
    to be that.\nI'm not Steve Yeagie, I'm not Simon Willison, I haven't been a developer
    for 20 years.\nI'm just a guy who learned some Python to solve some problems and
    happen to work faster than a lot of people and that reputation has really\nIt's
    treated me well for sure.\nAnd my disposition to problem solving has, up until
    recently, I felt like I had been, like I've been compensated well by my company,
    but then I've also been able to do more work outside of one job for me to feel
    the value of my own skills.\nBut now with AI changing everything,\nCan I show
    you what's on the back of my story?\nIt feels like I've given up the personal
    gains.\nOne second, Alia.\nSo I've given up personally realizing the benefit of
    my own skill.\nAnd now I am like in a position where\nCaterpillar, and only Caterpillar,
    is reaping the benefit of my new scheme in agentic engineering.\nAnd I think that's
    just ticking me off because I stayed with Catt to do something good for the world.\nAnd
    I think we can do something good for the world.\nBut I'm tired.\n\n## Notes and
    Summarization\n\n## Possible Titles\n\n- Agents, Responsibility, and the Cost
    of Going Faster\n- The Irony of 10x\n- More Output, Less Upside\n- When AI Made
    Me Faster and Poorer\n- Being Crushed by Responsibility\n\n## Working Angle\n\nThis
    should probably be a personal essay, not a manifesto about AI. The core tension:\n\n-
    agents made me dramatically more productive\n- that productivity increased the
    responsibility I carry\n- the financial upside I used to realize personally has
    gone down, not up\n\n## Section Stub: Opening Scene\n\nPrompt:\n\n- Start with
    a concrete moment. Maybe reviewing a massive PR from the intern / contractor side
    of the system.\n- What did I feel in that moment: confusion, dread, pressure,
    responsibility, skepticism?\n- What was the specific thought: \"I might own all
    of this later, even if I did not really produce or validate all of it myself.\"\n-
    Can I open with one sharp sentence that names the tension right away?\n\nPossible
    opener idea:\n\n\"Agentic coding has made me faster than ever, but it has also
    made me feel responsible for more than I can honestly hold in my head.\"\n\n###
    My Opener\n\nAgentic coding has been an exciting change to me over the last couple
    of months\nspecifically. I've been using AI tools for a few years now but something
    really\nshifted with Opus 4.5 as well as the tools/harnesses getting better and
    more\nuseful around the same time. I've been influenced by people like Simon Willison\nand
    Steve Yegge who have been on the forefront of agentic coding and [vibe\nengineering](https://simonwillison.net/2025/Oct/7/vibe-engineering/)
    since the\ndawn of ChatGPT and in my small circles of work I'm definitely on the
    bloodiest\nbleeding edge of the adoption of these practices. Are they the future?
    I don't\nknow - I tried to maintain a skeptical posture but until the bubble pops
    it's\nlooking like this is at least a direction the future of my line of work
    is\ngoing.\n\nThe tough part is mixing the new world of agentic coding with\ndevelopers
    on wildly different points in the spectrum of adoption and maturity.\nHere's a
    short anecdote about what I mean...\n\nIn a new project that I'm on, I've refactored/reimplemented
    a lot of legacy\ncode, producing about 140k lines of code, configuration, and\ndocs
    in about 2 months. It's an insane amount of \"product\" and I've done it\nentirely
    with agents. But I didn't do it with a handful of vanilla chat\nsessions like
    \"Hey Copilot, reimplement this API, no mistakes\". I've been building\nout my
    own process for using LLMs effectively. I have no actual idea besides my\nown
    experience if what I'm building is useful, but it feels pretty good -\nagents
    for planning, building, reviewing, testing, etc. My \"harness\" includes\nspecialized
    agents and opinionated development workflows\nto try to ensure that code is never
    one-shotted into production.\n\nThe catch though is that I'm working with a handful
    of developers and they are not\nearly-adopters or aggressive experimenters with
    these new agentic tools. Most\nof them are still in Steve Yegge's stage 2 or 3
    of AI coding as he's outlined\n[in this medium\narticle introducing\nGasTown](https://steve-yegge.medium.com/welcome-to-gas-town-4f25ee16dd04).
    They\nopen a chat session, and say yes or no to . I've been in-between stages\n6
    and 7 for a while now - managing multiple agentic sessions that themselves\nrun
    specialized subagents primiarly for context management, although I don't\nfeel
    quite ready for full blown stage 8 (agents running agnets running agents).\nSo
    this isn't me saying that I'm\nbetter than the guys I'm working with or anything,
    just the project we're on\ntogether is now being touched by people on wildly different
    ends of the agentic\ncoding spectrum.\n\nFinally the anecdote - I'm getting PRs
    from guys in stages 2 and 3,\nthousands of lines of code and config, that I know
    they are not necessarily\nexperts in, but they're producing that code with fairly
    vanilla practices. How\ndo I verify it? I know my own prompts, I know what agents
    generated my code, I know\nthe direction and prodding I gave as they're building,
    but all\nI know in a PR review from someone else is the diff - not how they tested
    it\n(unless of course there's tests, and there must be, but tests are only as
    good\nas the tests are...), not the steering they gave, not the manual UAT validation\nthey
    did... And ultimately I own the code they merge because chances are I\noutlast
    their contract with the team. It's kind of a scary thing to review and\naccept...
    do I trust my\nagentic practices to validate their work? I genuinely don't know,
    and I also\nliterally can't sleep at night.\n\n## Section Stub: This Is About
    Me, Not Everyone\n\nPrompt:\n\n- Add a strong caveat early: this is not prediction,
    prescription, or universal truth.\n- Name my context: big company, internal platform
    work, political environment, mixed team ownership, frontier models via Copilot.\n-
    What about my temperament matters here? Hyperfocus, initiative, problem solving,

    to not mishear?\n\n### My section about me\n\nI want to make sure to reiterate
    the context of this post and my thoughts -\nit's really about me. I'm not saying
    anything here is true for anyone else, I'm\nnot making predictions about tomorrow,
    and I'm not a prominent FOSS developer or a\nhigh-profile ex-FAANG engineer. I
    work at a big company, not a fast-paced\nstartup. I work on internal tooling,
    nothing that should even be seen outside\nour network. I'm also a self-taught
    developer, not a trained software engineer.\n\nAs far as AI goes, lately I'm mostly
    all in on Github Copilot\nCLI with a sprinkling of Opencode. I'm not using Claude
    Code or Codex which\nhave seem to have their own communities around plugins and
    usage.\n\nAnd since we're so focused on me right now, the important thing to keep
    as the\nbackdrop for this post is my temperament and make-up. I'm definitely made
    a\ncertain kind of way, not different than every single person necessarily, but\ndifferent
    enough, from enough people, to not blend in with the 9-5 guy who can\nlog out
    and not think about work until the morning (I'm not saying that's a\nstrength
    either, my boundary problems are for another time).\n\nI feel an aggressive burden
    to solve problems and own those solutions, call it\nwhite-knighting or a savior
    complex if you want, but I've got enough of a\nreputation at Caterpillar now (and
    anywhere else I've worked) to become a go-to\nperson for more than I think is
    necessarily appropriate. And with that burden\nI'll bring results - if a problem
    hooks into my brain it. will. be. solved. I\nprobably won't do the best most clean-code
    solution right out of the gate, but\nI'll do whatever I can to solve it.\n\n##
    Section Stub: Who I Was Before Agents\n\nPrompt:\n\n- Briefly describe my work
    history and self-understanding.\n- I am not formally trained, so what made me
    valuable anyway?\n- What does it mean that people were impressed before AI too?\n-
    Pull in relevant threads from `pages/blog/forge-ahead.md`: prior projects, practical

    God gave me that made me effective before this shift?\n\nQuestions to answer:\n\n-
    What kind of worker was I before agents entered my workflow?\n- What did I already
    know how to do well?\n- What part of my value came from focus rather than brilliance?\n\n###
    My section about me before agents\n\nThat is who I was before agents made code
    easy to produce. My first boss at\nCaterpillar used the phrase \"tenacious learner\"
    to describe me in several\nreviews. I kind of rejected the description because
    I basically refused to\nbelieve that I was really any different, any harder of
    a worker, than my peers.\nBut with almost a decade of experience in the corporate
    world, and some adult\nperspective on my life, I think it's accurate... I am a
    harder worker, to my\ndetriment sometimes and I need to put up some firmer boundaries.
    I'm not\nbrilliant but I can focus for a long time in the right circumstances.
    The gift\nof perseverence (or the curse of not being able to let something go,
    depending\non how you look at it) has led to blessing in my life in both reward
    and skill.\n\nAnd then AI came along and with another set of the right circumstances\ncatalyzed
    a new way to work.\n\n## Section Stub: Why I Leaned Into Agentic Coding\n\nPrompt:\n\n-
    Explain that I did not stumble into this accidentally; I chose to go hard.\n-
    Why did it feel natural to me to adopt agents aggressively?\n- What about the
    mission at Caterpillar Autonomy made the leap feel worth it?\n- Pull in `pages/blog/cat-autonomy-2-0.md`:
    I stayed because the work felt meaningful, not merely because it was interesting.\n-
    Mention harness engineering / Nexus / experimentation if it helps show this was
    a deliberate investment.\n\nQuestions to answer:\n\n- Why did I trust this direction
    enough to reorganize my work around it?\n- Was I chasing novelty, usefulness,
    recognition, mission, or all of the above?\n\n### My section on leaning into agents\n\nI
    jumped onto experimenting with AI coding tools as soon as they became\navailable,
    but mostly just tried vibe coding rather than using tools for real\nengineering
    work. I vibed up an API at one of my jobs that went into production\nway too early,
    with far too little validation, and it was scary to support it\nfrom then on out.
    That was before\nagents were quite as useful as they can be now, but that experience
    along with\na handful of other stepping stones began to give me confidence in
    using the AI\nas a tool, like my IDE is a tool, for producing **solutions** that
    take the form of\ncode.\n\nAs one-shot apps got better and better, and as I learned
    about scoping work\nmore appropriately for agentic tools my confidence in them
    grew. There's quite\na difference between \"Claude make me a todo app, no mistakes\"
    and scoping out a\nsolution in natural language, with some technical guardrails,
    and having agents\ntackle the implementation methodically.\n\n## Section Stub:
    What Actually Changed\n\nPrompt:\n\n- Describe the practical change in my day-to-day
    work.\n- I am no longer mainly writing code line by line; I am directing, reviewing,
    testing, and integrating generated work.\n- What changed about the quantity of
    output?\n- What changed about how coworkers and managers perceived me?\n- What
    changed about the kind of questions people started asking me?\n\nQuestions to
    answer:\n\n- When did I realize this was not just autocomplete, but a new mode
    of working?\n- What does \"enterprise-grade work fully written and produced by
    agents\" actually mean in practice?\n\n### My section on what actually changed\n\nWhat's
    actually changed for me is quite a lot... I haven't opened my IDE to\nseriously
    write code for months now. I've oscillated between Opencode and\nCopilot CLI,
    leaning moreso into Copilot since it's an approved tool at work\nand as of mid-February
    is quite good. Mentally I'm approaching problems with a\nlittle more thought on
    the front-end than before because prior to agents I\nwould think as I implemented.
    At the scale of work that I do, this was really\nfine - working on CLI utilities
    to solve simple problems, developing an\niterative testing cycle for each problem
    that allowed me to move fast, and once\nI found a groove I was cooking. But now
    I don't even need to find it, I open\nOpencode or Copilot CLI with my Planner
    agent, describe what I want to happen and have\nOpus or GPT scope out a plan for
    me. Usually there's some back and forth on\nfeature scoping, then I review a markdown
    file it produces, and once it looks\ndecent enough to me I say \"go\" and it goes.\n\nThat
    works a lot better than I even care to admit because at the same time as\nI've
    been leaning harder into agents, I've been building my own harness of\nsorts -
    not a replacement for Copilot CLI or a competitor to Opencode, but\nmoreso an
    opinionated workflow spine that I force agents into, oh and I'm\ndefining the
    agents, to give strict gates to the SDLC (software development\nlifecycle). Plan
    and implement is fine for a lot of things, and I do think it's\nonly getting better.
    My harness, mentioned a few times around here before,\ncalled Nexus, is a set
    of agents and rules that I want the code I'll be\nresponsible for to go through
    before it lands in production. That cycle isn't\ntoo complex, and there's only
    about 10,000 similar tools to Nexus on Github\ntrending right now. I've thought
    about dropping my idea and picking up\nsomething more popular, like\n[superpowers](https://www.github.com/obra/superpowers)
    but at the moment I'm\ncontinuing to develop on and lean into my own idea here.
    I keep saying a blog\npost is coming, but the high level of Nexus is that it's
    a task\ntracker with a CLI that agents use to advance a ticket through a plan
    -> build\n-> test -> review -> verify -> merge lifecycle that is almost exactly
    how I\nwould otherwise have solved a problem by hand. I think it needs work, I
    need to\nbe harder on TDD methodlogies with agents, and work on verification gating
    a\nbit more (shoutout to [showboat](https://github.com/simonw/showboat) by Simon\nWillison)
    but overall it's a system of thinking that I already participate in\nso I'm doing
    my best to farm out specific parts of my workflow to agents rather\nthan trying
    to one-shot enterprise problems and solutions.\n\nI've noticed that as I've developed
    Nexus out though, I lean on the agents for\nmore and more of my own thinking,
    and am trusting my problem solving\n**process** moreso than my actual problem
    solving abilities.\n\n## Section Stub: The Hidden Cost of Speed\n\nPrompt:\n\n-
    Write about the gap between output and understanding.\n- I can ship more, but
    I do not always know the code as intimately as hand-written systems I built before.\n-
    Testing is not the same as ownership.\n- Documentation existing is not the same
    as confidence.\n- What kinds of future problems does speed create?\n\nQuestions
    to answer:\n\n- What do I know I have not fully read?\n- What kinds of bugs or
    maintenance traps am I afraid I am creating?\n- What does it feel like to build
    something that works but still feels partially foreign?\n\n### My section on hidden
    cost\n\nThe cost of this increase in speed is a lack of familiarity - and the
    fallout\nof lack of familiarity is hard to express. There's also many facets to
    it.\nFor me, the first facet is that Nexus helps me move fast, but as I've leaned\ninto
    it for more and more of\nthe planning, I'm less and less familiar with the state
    of the code. I find\nmyself asking my reviewer agents in fresh sessions often
    to explain it to me,\nand thankfully they're usually consistent, but nonetheless
    I'm still not\nintimtaely familiar with the code. And on Nexus it's not a big
    deal, that's low stakes, it's just me and my workflow.\n\nI'm using Nexus + Copilot
    at work and that feels like higher\nstakes... I have my agents explain the status
    of our project and although they're\nalso somewhat consistent the thing that's
    scary is that other people are\nworking on that repo with me, and that's where
    another layer of complexity\nmanifests itself. If its' just me and my clankers,
    let's go all day long,\nrebuild, ask questions, etc... but I have other developers
    I rub shoulders with\nnow, and if they ask me a question what am I going to say?
    \"Hold on, let me prompt\nmy agent for you\" - it's LMGTFY on steroids. And the
    burden becomes if I\nfeel like I can own and support what those other developers
    push into the repo.\n\n## Section Stub: Responsibility Without Clear Boundaries\n\nPrompt:\n\n-
    This may be the emotional center before the money section.\n- Describe the contractor
    / intern dynamic and why it intensifies the burden.\n- Their output can become
    my long-term maintenance problem.\n- The organization sees me as someone who understands
    AI and agents, so responsibility pools onto me.\n- Ask whether the pressure is
    explicit or self-imposed, but note that the body feels it either way.\n\nQuestions
    to answer:\n\n- Who is actually accountable when AI-assisted code breaks six months
    from now?\n- What does it mean to be treated like the local expert when I do not
    feel like one?\n- Why is being noticed beginning to feel costly instead of rewarding?\n\n###
    My section on responsibility\n\nWhy do I own their work? Well for the third time,
    this is about me, and my\nsituation is that the other developers I work with presently
    are all contractors.\nTheir work agreement with Cat could end at any second, for
    practically any\nreason. That's technically true for me as well, seeing as how
    Cat is an\n\"at-will\" employer (they can fire anyone for no reason at any time).
    But the\nincentive structure isn't there for these guys who technically work for
    an\nagency... their bonuses aren't bigger (or even exist) if Cat performs well,\nthere's
    no extra vacation days in it for em (aye, contractors don't get\nvacation days
    anyways), and not that it's a personal problem - but they're\nbasically mercenaries
    out to the highest bidder and I happen to know of\n**multiple times where Cat
    lost a good person to a higher bidder**.\n\nSo this isn't really me trying to
    be negative about contractors at all, I'm\nhere for a pay-check as well but Cat
    at least gives me SOME incentive to work\nhard with the goal of compensation regardless
    of how altruistic I feel in my\nown circumstances.\n\nIt's also not all about
    the fact that the other guys aren't on Cat's primary\npayroll, I've dug myself
    quite a hole over the last 8-10 years, gaining a\nreputation that I think many
    would appreciate, but for me only increases the\nstress. I dont' need to parrot
    every accolade I've ever received, that's not\nthe point, but to make the point
    as clear as I can - I have a\nlot of respect from quite a few people at Caterpillar.
    I'm blessed to have that\nreputation, and it's not like I haven't worked hard
    for it - but people talk\nabout me in a way so flattering I feel like the main
    character in a fictional\nstory sometimes.\n\nIn a fictional story I can check
    out the ending, hit up spark notes, or ask AI\nhow it ends... but there isn't
    an \"end\" in my real world scenario, there's only\ntomorrow and I feel the pressure
    of not knowing what tomorrow holds now more\nthan ever.\n\nBeing noticed is starting
    to feel more costly than rewarding...\n\n## Section Stub: The Financial Irony\n\nPrompt:\n\n-
    State this clearly and concretely.\n- For years, I realized more value from my
    output because I worked two jobs concurrently.\n- Due to the new role, new responsibility,
    and the demands of agentic coding in this environment, I had to go down to one
    job.\n- So the irony is: the tools make me dramatically more productive, but I
    personally realize less economic upside from my own gifts and skills than I did
    before.\n- Keep this grounded and honest, not whiny.\n\nQuestions to answer:\n\n-
    What exactly have I lost financially?\n- Why does this feel bigger than \"I want
    more money\"?\n- Where do the gains of AI go when an individual contributor gets
    faster?\n- If I can now produce 10x more code, why does my life not feel lighter,
    freer, or better compensated?\n\nPossible thesis sentence:\n\n\"Agentic coding
    increased the value I create at work, while decreasing the share of that value
    I personally get to realize.\"\n\n### My section on cost\n\nWhat's the cost? It's
    hard to get specific without writing a novel but here's\nthe TLDR - because I've
    been pretty good at what I do I've been able to do this\ntype of work outside
    my normal 9-5 responsibilities and with that extra work\nhas been some pretty
    great financial benefits. However with Cat changes,\nresponsibility increases,
    and now owning code that others (and their clankers)\nwrite, the extra time I
    gained for myself is eaten-up and has been reclaimed by\nthe mega-corp... \"Exceeding
    expectations\" every year just meant the bar is\nraised, the expectations are
    higher, the time-commitment requirement is higher,\nand as I've had to meet the
    requirements of both the new world and the curse of\nbeing noticed, I've lost
    the time for the extra work... For years I've realized\nthe benefit of my own
    skills and drive, but the irony of agents (and a handful\nof other things) is
    that with the dramatic increase in expectations, not only\non me but on those
    I work with and therefore their output, I don't get to\nrealize the benefits of
    my own gifts anymore.\n\n## Section Stub: Mission, Meaning, and Fatigue\n\nPrompt:\n\n-
    Reaffirm the good: this is meaningful work, and that matters to me.\n- Tie back
    to autonomy, safety, and the reasons I stayed at Caterpillar.\n- Then tell the
    truth: meaning does not cancel fatigue.\n- What has already started to feel tired,
    dulled, or overdrawn in me?\n\nQuestions to answer:\n\n- What purpose did this
    role awaken in me?\n- Why does that sense of purpose already feel threatened?\n-
    How do I talk honestly about fatigue without pretending I am fully burned out
    if I am not sure?\n\n### My section on meaning\n\nI feel very torn because the
    work I've been called into with Cat is good, I\nsaid in [[cat-autonomy-2-0]] that
    autonomy will save people's lives. I love\ngetting to participate in that mission,
    it's the primary reason I didn't jump\nship to try to maintain the levels, and
    type, of work I was doing before... But in a few\nshort months the mission is
    being drowned out by expectations and requirements\nthat are so high I'm losing
    the grip on my own life.\n\n## Section Stub: Questions I Cannot Yet Answer\n\nPrompt:\n\n-
    Let the essay breathe here instead of forcing a neat conclusion.\n- Gather the
    real unresolved questions.\n- If I stopped using agents, would I still impress
    people?\n- Would I solve fewer problems or create fewer future ones?\n- Am I carrying
    real expectations or anticipated ones?\n- Is this making me a better engineer,
    or just a faster source of output and responsibility?\n\n### My section on questions\n\nThat
    leads me to questions that I can't answer, the question I ask daily now of\n\"What
    about tomorrow?\". What will agents do for us tomorrow, what problems will\nbe
    solved, what bugs will I create (by agents of course because I've never\nwritten
    a bug by hand in my whole life \\s). If I stopped using agents would\npeople still
    be impressed? Would it even matter?\n\n## Section Stub: Conclusion\n\nPrompt:\n\n-
    End modestly, not with a manifesto.\n- I am not anti-AI.\n- I am not saying agents
    are bad.\n- I am trying to tell the truth about what this transition has cost
    me personally.\n- Land on the mismatch between speed, responsibility, and reward.\n\nPossible
    closing direction:\n\n- The tools are real.\n- The gains are real.\n- The responsibility
    is real.\n- The redistribution of value is also real.\n- I am still trying to
    understand what faithfulness looks like inside that tension.\n\n### My conclusion\n\nI'm
    certainly not anti-AI, it's typing all my code. I'm not anti-collaboration, although
    I do wish I could work alone with just my clanker-army to worry about. I'm not
    sure what I am anymore though... AI has changed how I work, what I work on, and
    who I work with... everything has changed in such a short period of time and like
    the ending of this post, it's pretty jarring.\n\nDeath comes to us all - James
    Acaster.\nThanks for reading.\n\n## Material To Weave In\n\n- My lack of formal
    software engineering background\n- My history of being useful by solving practical
    problems quickly\n- My tendency toward hyperfocus and over-responsibility\n- The
    shift from hand-written confidence to agent-mediated uncertainty\n- The contractor
    / intern maintenance concern\n- The drop from two incomes to one\n- The irony
    that Cat benefits more from my increased output than I do\n- The Christian framing
    around calling, providence, stewardship, and fatigue\n\n## Things To Avoid\n\n-
    sounding like I am making universal claims about all AI use\n- sounding bitter
    without being specific\n- centering line-count metrics too much without translating
    them into responsibility\n- pretending I have fully landed on a conclusion\n-
    making this a complaint post about coworkers instead of a reflection on burden
    and change\n"
